Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
7 min
The Mountaintop Scenario and Al-Ash'ari's Position
The author discusses the mountaintop case and then details al-Ash'ari's position on the muqallid's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the later scholars' discussion about the status of a person who grows up without reflection and the detailed view of al-Ash'ari on the faith of the muqallid.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari considers the muqallid a believer with tasdiq but imperfect, not a disbeliever.

The third part of the passage moves into a more specific discussion: the case of someone who grew up on a mountaintop without any reflection, later hears the truth and affirms it. The later scholars debate whether such a person is punished as a disbeliever or not. Then the author presents the views of those who say that a muqallid's faith is not valid or beneficial, culminating in al-Ash'ari's well-known position and its clarification by al-Baghdadi. This lesson clarifies the final position.

Guided Path
1
Read the mountaintop scenario
Understand the question: does a person raised in isolation who affirms the truth when informed get punished as a disbeliever?
2
Note the two answers
Some say yes (because ignorance), others say no (because tasdiq nullifies punishment).
3
Identify the two groups on muqallid's faith
One group allows belief based on authority; the other (al-Ash'ari's) requires rational evidence per issue but not the ability to express it.
4
Understand al-Baghdadi's clarification
Al-Ash'ari's denial of faith is for perfect faith; the muqallid with tasdiq is still a believer, though sinful.
5
Reflect on the resolution
The apparent disagreement with al-Ash'ari is resolved: he only denies perfect faith, not faith itself.

According to al-Baghdadi's clarification, what is the status of a muqallid who has tasdiq but no nazar?
A) He is a disbeliever

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to be a believer, but have you ever truly reflected on the proofs for God's existence and prophethood as outlined in this lesson? ?

?
Al-Ash'ari teaches that neglecting nazar is a sin, even if one has tasdiq. Do you feel any personal accountability for not investing time in studying the rational proofs of your faith? What evidence from creation or revelation will you examine this week to strengthen your certainty?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

The Mu'tazila View on Iman and Argumentation
Sanusi presents and refutes the Mu'tazila definition of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila requirement that faith be based on argumentative ability, their etymological definition of iman, and the refutation of that definition.

Core Idea
True faith is firm belief, not merely the ability to argue from evidence.

The Mu'tazila argued that genuine faith requires not only belief but also the ability to defend it through rational argument, even going so far as to declare those incapable of such argument as disbelievers. In this passage, Sanusi presents their position and then refutes their etymological argument that iman means 'security' attained only through knowledge. This lesson walks through that key debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Read the opening to find what the Mu'tazila require for faith regarding argumentation and evidence.
They demand the ability to argue with opponents and resolve difficulties.
2
Note the two interpretive bases
Sanusi gives two possible reasons for their requirement: one about major sins, another about sufficiency of affirmation. Understand both.
The first relates to the issue of the major sinner (sahib al-kabira).
3
Examine the etymological argument
Read how the Mu'tazila derive iman from amn (security) and claim it requires knowledge.
They argue iman means entering into security, which they say only comes through knowledge.
4
See the refutation
Understand how Sanusi refutes: the derivation relates to the informant, and security is achieved by firm belief even through taqlid.
This shows that argumentative ability is not necessary for true iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : لا بد من ابتناء الاعتقاد على الدليل من الاقتدار على مجادلة الخصوم، وحل ما يورد عليه من الإشكال وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة، ولم يحكموا بإيمان من عجز عن شيء من ذلك، بل حكم أبو هاشم بكفره ، فإن بنوا ذلك على أن ترك النظر كبيرة تخرج من الإيمان إذا طرأت وتمنع من الدخول فيه إذا قارنت فهي مسألة صاحب الكبيرة ، وقد سبقت. وإن أرادوا أن مثل هذا التصديق لا يكفي في الإيمان أو لا ينفع فمسألة أخرى ، وبهذا يشعر تمسكاتهم وهي وجوه . الأول - أن حقيقة الإيمان إدخال النفس في الأمان من أن يكون مكذوباً ومخدوعاً وملتبساً عليه، على أنه إفعال من الأمن للتعدية أو للصيرورة كأنه صار ذا أمن، وذلك إنما يكون بالعلم ورد بأنه يجعل متعلقا بالمخبر مثل : به، وله لا بالسامع. فالمناسب عند ملاحظة الاشتقاق من الأمن أن يقال : معناه آمنه المخالفة والتكذيب على ما صرح به المعتزلة وذلك بالتصديق سواء كان عن دليل ، أو لا . ولو سلم فالأمن من أن يكون مكذوباً أو مخدوعاً يحصل بالاعتقاد الجازم، وإن كان عن تقليد . الثاني.
And among them are those who said: it is necessary that belief be based on evidence, including the ability to argue with opponents and resolve any difficulties presented, and this is the view of the Mu'tazila. They did not rule that one who is incapable of anything of that is a believer; rather, Abu Hashim ruled him a disbeliever. If they base that on the view that abandoning nazar is a major sin that removes one from faith if it occurs and prevents entry into it if concurrent, then it is the issue of the major sinner, which has preceded. And if they intend that such tasdiq (affirmation) is not sufficient for faith or does not benefit, then it is another issue. And their arguments suggest this, and they are several: First, that the reality of iman (faith) is entering the soul into security from being lied to, deceived, or confused; on the basis that it is a verbal noun (if'al) from amn (security) for transitivity or becoming, as if he becomes one with security, and that is only through knowledge. It is refuted that it makes the object related to the informant (e.g., 'to him' or 'for him'), not to the hearer. So the appropriate thing when considering the derivation from amn is to say: its meaning is 'he gave him security from contradiction and denial' as stated by the Mu'tazila, and that is through tasdiq, whether from evidence or not. And even if conceded, security from being lied to or deceived is attained by firm belief, even if through taqlid. Second.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the Mu'tazila view on the requirement for argumentation and their definition of iman. The author begins by stating that a third group (the Mu'tazila) requires belief to be based on evidence including the ability to argue with opponents and resolve difficulties. They do not consider anyone incapable of this to be a believer; Abu Hashim even declares such a person a disbeliever. The author notes that if this position is based on the idea that abandoning nazar is a major sin that excludes from faith, then it is the issue of the major sinner (sahib al-kabira), which was already discussed. But if they mean that such simple affirmation (tasdiq) is insufficient for faith or beneficial, then it is a separate issue. The author then presents the first of their arguments (the etymological definition of iman): they claim that iman literally means 'entering the soul into security from being lied to, deceived, or confused' because it is derived from amn (security) with an if'al pattern indicating transitivity or becoming, and that this security can only come through knowledge. The refutation is that the derivation actually makes the object the informant (e.g., 'he gave him security'), not the hearer. The correct meaning from the root is 'he gave him security from contradiction and denial,' which is achieved through tasdiq (affirmation), whether based on evidence or not. Even if we accept their definition, security from being deceived is attained by firm belief (i'tiqad jazim), even if through taqlid. This refutes their claim that iman requires knowledge derived from nazar. The passage ends with 'Second,' indicating the beginning of another argument, which is not fully given in this window.

Misconception: Iman requires the ability to debate and defend one's faith rationally; without that, a person is not a true believer.
Correction: Iman is firm belief (i'tiqad jazim), which can be attained through taqlid and does not require argumentative skill. The Mu'tazila definition is refuted.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila requirement for argumentative ability is refuted by showing that the definition of iman as security is satisfied by firm belief, regardless of whether it is based on evidence or taqlid.

Al-Ash'ari's View on the Believer Who Abandons Reflection
Al-Ash'ari's ruling on one who has tasdiq but abandons nazar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's nuanced position that a person with tasdiq who neglects nazar is not a disbeliever but a disobedient believer, and why this does not involve the intermediate position.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari affirms tasdiq as sufficient to avoid kufr, but nazar is a duty whose neglect is sinful.

This passage clarifies al-Ash'ari's stance on a crucial question: what is the status of someone who believes in their heart but never engages in theological reflection? Al-Ash'ari distinguishes himself from both the Mu'tazila and other positions by affirming that such a person is not a kafir, yet not a perfect believer. This subtlety is key to understanding later debates on faith and works.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ash'ari's ruling
Read the first sentence carefully: 'he is not, according to al-Ash'ari, a believer absolutely; he is not a disbeliever...' Notice the precise wording.
2
Understand the reasons
Identify why he is not a kafir (tasdiq exists) and why he is disobedient (abandoned nazar).
3
See the implication
The phrase 'not a perfect believer' is linked to the case of abandoning actions, clarifying that al-Ash'ari does not hold the intermediate position.
4
Conclude the disagreement
The author notes that this shows no real disagreement with al-Ash'ari on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكن عند الأشعري مؤمناً على الإطلاق، فليس بكافر لوجود التصديق، لكنه عاص بتركه النظر والاستدلال فيعفو الله عنه أو يعذبه بقدر ذنبه وعاقبته الجنة . وهذا يشعر بأن مراد الأشعري أنه لا يكون مؤمناً على الكمال، كما في ترك الأعمال، وإلا فهو لا يقول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين، ولا بدخول غير المؤمن الجنة، وعند هذا يظهر أنه لا خلاف معه على التحقيق، ومنهم من قال : لا بد من ابتناء الاعتقاد على الدليل من الاقتدار على مجادلة الخصوم، وحل ما يورد عليه من الإشكال وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة، ولم يحكموا بإيمان من عجز عن شيء من ذلك، بل حكم أبو هاشم بكفره ، فإن بنوا ذلك على أن ترك النظر كبيرة تخرج من الإيمان إذا طرأت وتمنع من الدخول فيه إذا قارنت فهي مسألة صاحب الكبيرة ، وقد سبقت. وإن أرادوا أن مثل هذا التصديق لا يكفي في الإيمان أو لا ينفع فمسألة أخرى ، وبهذا يشعر تمسكـاتهم وهي وجوه .
He is not, according to al-Ash'ari, a believer absolutely; he is not a disbeliever due to the existence of tasdiq, but he is disobedient by abandoning nazar and istidlal, so God may forgive him or punish him according to his sin, and his final abode is Paradise. And this indicates that al-Ash'ari's intent is that he is not a perfect believer, as in the case of abandoning actions; otherwise, he does not hold the intermediate position between the two positions, nor the entry of a non-believer into Paradise. So it appears that there is no real disagreement with him. And among them (the Mu'tazila) some said: belief must be based on evidence for the ability to argue with opponents and solve the doubts raised against them; this is the view of the Mu'tazila, and they did not judge as believers those incapable of that; rather, Abu Hashim judged him as a disbeliever. If they base that on the view that abandoning nazar is a major sin that expels from iman if it occurs (after iman) and prevents entry into it if concurrent, then it is the issue of the grave sinner (sahib al-kabira), which has preceded. And if they intend that such tasdiq is not sufficient for iman or does not benefit, then it is another issue. And this is indicated by their arguments, and they are aspects (wujuh).
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage begins by clarifying al-Ash'ari's position on a person who possesses tasdiq (affirmation of faith) but does not engage in nazar (reflection) and istidlal (inference). According to al-Ash'ari, such a person is not a kafir because tasdiq is present, but he is disobedient for abandoning the obligation of nazar. His ultimate fate rests with God. This indicates al-Ash'ari means he is not a perfect believer, similar to someone who neglects actions. The author then distinguishes two distinct issues raised by the Mu'tazila who require evidence for belief: first, the issue of whether abandoning nazar is a major sin that expels from iman—this is the topic of the grave sinner (sahib al-kabira), already discussed; second, the issue of whether tasdiq without evidence is insufficient for iman—this is the topic at hand. The author states that the following arguments (wujuh) pertain to the second issue.

Two Issues: Major Sin vs Insufficiency of Taqlid
Distinguishing the Mu'tazila's two objections against taqlid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between the Mu'tazila's two arguments against taqlid: one treating abandonment of nazar as a major sin, and the other claiming that tasdiq without evidence is insufficient for iman.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's objection to taqlid rests on two distinct issues: one about major sin, the other about the nature of faith itself.

The Mu'tazila demand that belief be based on evidence, but their reasoning can be understood through two different lenses. This passage clearly separates the issue of whether abandoning nazar is a major sin that expels from faith from the deeper question of whether a conviction lacking rational evidence is truly iman. Understanding this distinction is crucial for following the subsequent arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's requirement
Read: 'belief must be based on evidence for the ability to argue with opponents...' Note the practical criterion.
2
Identify the two bases
The passage presents two possible grounds: (a) abandoning nazar is a major sin (kabira) that expels from iman, and (b) tasdiq without evidence is insufficient.
3
Relate to previous discussion
The author notes that the first ground relates to the already-discussed issue of the grave sinner (sahib al-kabira).
4
Focus on the second issue
The second ground is the new issue: does such tasdiq count as iman? This is the subject of the coming arguments.
5
Recognize the structure
The final sentence says the following arguments (wujuh) pertain to this second issue.

First Argument on Iman and Its Refutation
First argument of those requiring evidence for iman and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that iman requires knowledge and how its refutation relies on etymology and the concept of tasdiq.

Core Idea
Iman does not require proof; firm conviction alone suffices.

This lesson presents the first objection against the sufficiency of taqlid for iman. The objector defines iman as a state of safety that can only be achieved through certain knowledge. The response, however, deconstructs this definition by analyzing the Arabic derivation and showing that tasdiq (affirmation) is the core, regardless of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of argument and response.
2
Analyze the etymological argument
Notice how the response uses the root 'amn' to argue that iman relates to the informant, not the listener. This shifts the requirement from knowledge to tasdiq.
The term 'amana' is transitive to the informant (mukhbar).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Even if the definition were accepted, the response concedes that safety from falsity is achieved by firm conviction (i'tiqad jazim), which can come from taqlid.
This shows that the argument against taqlid fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - أن حقيقة الإيمان إدخال النفس في الأمان من أن يكون مكذوباً ومخدوعاً وملتبساً عليه، على أنه إفعال من الأمن للتعدية أو للصيرورة كأنـه صار ذا أمن، وذلك إنما يكون بالعلم ورد بأنه يجعل متعلقا بالمخبر مثل: به، وله لا بالسامع. فالمناسب عند ملاحظة الاشتقاق من الأمن أن يقال : معناه آمنه المخالفة والتكذيب على ما صرح به المعتزلة وذلك بالتصديق سواء كان عن دليل ، أو لا . ولو سلم فالأمن(۱) من أن يكون مكذوباً أو مخدوعاً يحصل بالاعتقاد الجازم، وإن كان عن تقليد .
The first: that the reality of iman is to bring oneself into safety from being falsified, deceived, or confounded, considering that it is a verbal form from amn for transitive or for becoming, as if he becomes one with security, and that is only achieved by knowledge. It is responded: this makes it relate to the informant, like 'to him' and 'for him', not to the listener. So the appropriate implication from the derivation from amn is that it means to secure from contradiction and falsification, as the Mu'tazila stated, and that is by tasdiq, whether based on evidence or not. And if it is granted, then the security from being falsified or deceived is attained by firm conviction, even if by taqlid.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (wajh) of those who claim that taqlid is insufficient for iman. The argument defines iman as 'entering into safety' (idkhal al-nafs fi al-aman) from being falsified or deceived, which requires knowledge ('ilm). The response refutes this by analyzing the etymology: the verb 'amana' is transitive to the informant (al-mukhbar), not the listener. Thus the appropriate meaning is that the believer secures the informant from being contradicted—this is achieved by tasdiq (affirmation) whether with evidence or not. Even if the definition were accepted, the safety from falsity comes from firm conviction (i'tiqad jazim), even if acquired through taqlid.

Key Terms
إيمان
أمن
تصديق
علم
تقليد
اعتقاد جازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of iman as 'safety from falsity' automatically requires knowledge.
Correction: The response shows that safety from falsity is achieved by tasdiq (affirmation), which does not necessarily require evidence-based knowledge; firm conviction suffices.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the response to the first argument that iman requires knowledge?
A) Iman is only achieved by inference
B) The derivation of iman shows it relates to the informant, not the listener
C) Firm conviction is impossible without evidence
D) Taqlid always leads to error

Key Takeaway
Iman is fundamentally tasdiq, and safety from falsity is achieved by firm conviction, not necessarily through knowledge derived from evidence.

The Five Types of Iman
Definition of al-amn and classification of iman into five types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that iman is not a single state but is classified into five distinct types according to the person possessing it.

Core Idea
Iman varies: angels have innate iman, prophets protected iman, believers accepted iman, innovators suspended iman, hypocrites rejected iman.

The passage begins by defining a related concept, al-amn (security), then immediately presents a classification of iman into five types. This teaches us that iman is not a monolithic category; its nature differs based on the individual. As you read, note how each type is tied to a specific group and what that reveals about their spiritual state.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author first defines ‘al-amn’ (security) before classifying iman. This shows the link between security of the heart and faith.
Al-amn is not the main topic but provides a foundation.
2
Identify the five types
List the five types: innate (matbu'), accepted (maqbul), protected (ma'sum), suspended (mawquf), and rejected (mardud). Match each to its group.
The groups are angels, believers, prophets, innovators, and hypocrites respectively.
3
Reflect on the differences
Consider why innovators have ‘suspended’ iman and hypocrites have ‘rejected’ iman. This shows that iman can be incomplete or insincere, affecting its validity.
4
Check your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to test your recall of which type belongs to which group.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) الأمن : هو عدم توقع مكروه في الزمان الآتي ، والإيمان على خمسة أوجه : إيمان مطبوع ، وإيمان مقبول، وإيمان معصوم، وإيمان موقوف، وإيمان مردود، فالإيمان المطبوع هو إيمان الملائكة، والإيمان المعصوم هو إيمان الأنبياء والإيمان المقبول : هو إيمان المؤمنين والإيمان الموقوف : هو إيمان المبتدعين، والإيمان :المردود : هو إيمان المنافقين.
(1) al-Amn: absence of expectation of harm in the future. And iman is of five kinds: innate iman, accepted iman, protected iman, suspended iman, and rejected iman. The innate iman is the iman of angels; the protected iman is the iman of prophets; the accepted iman is the iman of believers; the suspended iman is the iman of innovators (mubtadi'in); and the rejected iman is the iman of hypocrites.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage defines al-amn (security) as a state of not expecting harm in the future. It then categorizes iman into five distinct types based on the possessor: innate (matbu')—the iman of angels, who were created with it; protected (ma'sum)—the iman of prophets, who are safeguarded from sin; accepted (maqbul)—the iman of believers, i.e., the righteous; suspended (mawquf)—the iman of innovators (mubtadi'in), whose fate is pending; and rejected (mardud)—the iman of hypocrites, which is not accepted. This classification reinforces the nuanced understanding of iman beyond a single category.

Misconception: That iman is a single, uniform state that is the same for all believers.
Correction: The passage shows that iman has five distinct types depending on the person: angels have innate faith, prophets have protected faith, believers have accepted faith, innovators have suspended faith, and hypocrites have rejected faith. Thus, iman varies in its nature and acceptance.

Key Takeaway
Iman is not monolithic; it has five types, each specific to a certain class of people, from angels to hypocrites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Argument: Circularity of Taqlid and Its Refutation
Third argument against taqlid with refutation and clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against taqlid (that it leads to circularity or contradiction) and how the response reframes the debate to matters already proven by evidence, thus dismissing the argument as a fallacy.

Core Idea
The third argument fails because it ignores that the source's truth is already known by evidence.

The third argument attempts to show that taqlid of a true source is either circular or contradictory. The response, however, clarifies that the discussion is not about whether the source is true, but about matters already established by evidence from Islam. The objection is dismissed as a fallacy, and the real point—that taqlid does not suffice for obligatory knowledge—is already settled by text and consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument and response.
Notice the conditional structure: if the source is false... if it is true...
2
Identify the two branches of the argument
The argument splits the source (al-asl) into false (yielding obvious false imitation) and true. For the true source, it says truth is known either by imitation (circular) or by evidence (contradictory). Understand why each is problematic.
3
Understand the response and final point
The response redefines the debate: we are talking about matters already known by evidence to be from Islam. The claim of circularity/contradiction is a fallacy. Then the author notes that the real issue—taqlid not fulfilling obligation—is already agreed upon, so these weak arguments are unnecessary.
The final sentence underscores that the correct position is established by Qur'an and consensus on the obligation of nazar and istidlal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن الأصل الذي يقلد فيه إن كان باطلا، فتقليـده باطل بالاتفاق كتقليد اليهود والنصارى والمجوس وعبدة الأوثان أسلافهم. وإن كان حقاً فحقيته إما أن يعلم بالتقليد فدور، أو بالدليل فتناقض . ورد بأن الكلام فيما علم حقيته بالدليل كالأحكام التي علم كونها من دين الإسلام أن من هل يكون مؤمنا يجري عليه أحكام المؤمنين في الدنيا والآخرة، وإن بتركه النظر والاستدلال وأما ما يقال إن القول بجواز التقليد (١) إن لم يكن عن دليل فباطل وإن كان فتناقض . فمغالطة ظاهرة لا يقال : المقصود أن التقليد لا يكفي في الخروج عن عهدة الواجب فيما وجب العلم به من أصول الإسلام وبعض هذه الوجوه يفيد ذلك، لأنا نقول : هذا مما لا نزاع فيه ولا حاجة به إلى هذه الوجوه الضعيفة لثبوته بالنص والإجماع على وجوب النظر والاستدلال
The third: that the original (authority) which is imitated, if it is false, then imitating it is false by agreement, like the imitation of Jews, Christians, Magians, and idolaters of their forefathers. And if it is true, then its truth is either known by imitation, which is circular, or by evidence, which is contradictory. It is responded that the discussion is about what is known by evidence to be true, such as the rulings known to be from the religion of Islam: does a person become a believer on whom the rulings of believers apply in this world and the Hereafter, despite his abandoning nazar and istidlal? And as for the statement that the permissibility of imitation (1) if not based on evidence is false, and if based on evidence then it is a contradiction, it is an obvious fallacy. It is not said: the purpose is that imitation does not suffice to fulfill the obligation regarding what is obligatory to know from the fundamentals of Islam, and some of these arguments indicate that. For we say: this is something about which there is no dispute, and there is no need for these weak arguments, because it is established by the text and consensus on the obligation of nazar and istidlal.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument: the source (al-asl) that one imitates is either false (so imitation is false) or true. If true, its truth is known either by imitation (circular) or by evidence (contradiction—because if known by evidence, it is not pure imitation). The response redefines the debate: the discussion concerns matters already known by evidence to be from Islam (like rulings); the question is whether the person who accepts them without personally performing nazar is a believer. The argument that 'taqlid without evidence is false, with evidence is contradiction' is dismissed as a fallacy. The author then clarifies that the issue is not whether taqlid suffices for obligatory knowledge (that is already agreed upon by text and consensus), but whether these weak arguments are needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the third argument proves that taqlid is impossible in all cases because any true source leads to circularity or contradiction.
Correction: The response clarifies that the argument only applies to sources whose truth is not already established by evidence. For matters already known by evidence (like Islamic rulings), the question is whether accepting them via taqlid suffices for faith—not about the source's truth.

Checkpoint
How does the response refute the third argument's claim that taqlid of a true source leads to a contradiction?
A) By denying that the source is true
B) By stating the discussion concerns matters already known by evidence to be from Islam
C) By claiming that taqlid is never circular
D) By asserting that all imitation is false

Key Takeaway
The third argument against taqlid fails because it misrepresents the scope of the debate; the real issue is settled by nass and ijmāʿ without need for such weak reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a core Islamic belief because your parents or community taught it, do you feel that personal accountability to verify it with evidence, or do you rely on their certainty? This lesson shows that even if the source is true, blind imitation is circular. Where are you most vulnerable to this circularity, and what will you do this week to break it?
?
The passage says that the obligation to use reason (nazzar) is established by nass and ijmāʿ—it is not optional. Have you ever treated the duty to personally investigate the fundamentals of faith as something for scholars only? If you took that duty upon yourself today, what is the first belief you would seek to prove by evidence?
?
The third argument is dismissed as an 'obvious fallacy' because it misapplies the scope. How often do you fall into the trap of arguing against a position without understanding its actual context? This lesson warns against superficial reasoning. When you discuss matters of creed with others, do you first clarify the scope, or do you assume your argument is decisive?

Mu'tazili Views on Taqlid for the Incapable
Reporting Mu'tazili views on taqlid for the incapable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some Mu'tazili scholars exempted certain groups from the obligation of nazar and instead obligated them to taqlid.

Core Idea
Even among Mu'tazila, nazar was not required of everyone; the incapable were obligated to imitate the truthful.

While the mainstream Mu'tazili position required nazar for all believers, a minority view attributed to al-Ka'bi and others held that the common people, slaves, and many women were incapable of examining evidences. Instead, they were obligated to follow reliable scholars and form correct conjectures. This lesson explores that alternative position and its nuanced conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full English translation. Notice the key terms like تقليد, ظن, and نظر.
The passage reports two distinct views: one from al-Ka'bi's group and one from later scholars.
2
Identify the two groups of people
The passage divides people into 'people of nazar' (rational, capable) and those obligated to taqlid (common people, slaves, many women). Mark how each group's obligation differs.
3
Understand the conditions on taqlid
Note that taqlid is not unrestricted: the incapable must imitate the truthful (muhaqq) and have correct conjecture (zann sawab). Also note the later addition about hearing initial proofs.
The later scholars added a third category: if even hearing proofs is impossible, there is no obligation at all.
4
Contrast with the mainstream Mu'tazili view
Reflect on how this view differs from the more common Mu'tazili insistence on nazar for all. This shows internal diversity within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه حكي عن الكعبي (٢) وابن أبي عياش و جمع آخر من المعتزلة أن العقلاء من من كلف النظر، وهم أرباب النظر، كلف التقليد والظن وهم العوام ، والعبيد، وكثير من النسوان لعجزهم عن النظر في الأدلة وتمييزها عن الشبه. لكنهم كلفوا تقليد المحق دون المبطل . والظن الصائب دون الخطأ . وذكر بعض المتأخرين منهم أن العاجزين كلفوا أن يسمعوا أوائل الدلائل التي تتسارع إلى الإفهام . فإن فهموا كفاهم وهم أصحاب الجمل، ولا يكلفون تلخيص العبارة، وإن لم يمكنهم الوقوف عليها، فليسوا مكلفين أصلا، وإنما خلقوا
Moreover, it is reported from al-Ka'bi (2), Ibn Abi 'Ayyash, and another group of the Mu'tazila that the rational people among those obligated to nazar, and they are the people of nazar, are obligated to taqlid and conjecture, and they are the common people, slaves, and many women due to their inability to examine evidences and distinguish them from doubts. However, they are obligated to imitate the truthful, not the false, and correct conjecture, not error. And some later scholars among them mentioned that the incapable are obligated to hear the first proofs that quickly lead to understanding. If they understand, that suffices for them, and they are the people of summary, and they are not obligated to elaborate the expression. And if they cannot grasp them, they are not obligated at all, and they were only created [for something else].
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage reports a different Mu'tazili view attributed to al-Ka'bi, Ibn Abi 'Ayyash, and others. They held that while rational people (al-'uqala') are the 'people of nazar' and are obligated to nazar, the common people ('awamm), slaves, and many women are obligated to taqlid (imitation) and zann (conjecture) because they are incapable of examining evidences. However, they must imitate the truthful (muhaqq) and have correct conjecture. Later scholars added that the incapable are only obligated to hear initial proofs that are easily understood; if they understand, it suffices; if not, they have no obligation at all. This shows a nuanced view that the obligation of nazar is not universal.

نظر
دلائل
عوام
عبيد
نسوان
محق
مبطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila required nazar of every human being without exception.
Correction: This passage shows that some Mu'tazila actually exempted common people, slaves, and many women from nazar, obligating them to taqlid instead.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi, who are obligated to taqlid rather than nazar?
A) Rational people only
B) Common people, slaves, and many women
C) Only scholars
D) All believers

Key Takeaway
Some Mu'tazila held that the incapable are obligated to imitate the truthful and form correct conjecture, not to perform independent nazar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel incapable of understanding religious proofs? How does this passage challenge or comfort you regarding your own obligation to seek knowledge? What is one step you can take this week to increase your ability to examine your beliefs?
?
The Mu'tazila said the incapable must imitate the truthful. Who are the 'truthful' you are imitating in your faith? Are you confident they are truly truthful? What criteria do you use to determine whom to trust in religious matters?
?
This passage implies that obligation is based on ability. Reflect: Are you maximizing your ability in seeking certainty in your beliefs, or are you relying on taqlid when you could do more? What step will you take this week to move from taqlid to greater certainty?

Definition of Taqlid
Definition of taqlid as following without evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical meaning of taqlid and why it is defined by the absence of personal reasoning.

Core Idea
Taqlid means following without evidence—a 'necklace' of blind acceptance.

In this lesson, we examine the precise definition of taqlid as given by classical scholars. Understanding this definition is essential for grasping later discussions on whether taqlid can be a valid basis for faith. The term itself evokes the image of placing a necklace around one's neck, symbolizing uncritical adherence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two parts: the literal meaning and the technical definition.
2
Understand the literal meaning
The word 'taqlid' literally means 'to place a necklace' (qilada). Reflect on how this metaphor relates to following another blindly.
3
Grasp the technical definition
Technically, taqlid is following someone's statement or action while believing it true, but without any personal observation (nazar) or reflection (tammul) on evidence.
The absence of nazar and tammul is key.
4
Compare with the alternative definition
The passage also gives a shorter definition: 'saying of another without proof or evidence.' Compare this with the longer version.
Both emphasize lack of personal verification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) التقليد : عبارة عن اتباع الإنسان غيره فيما يقول أو يفعل معتقداً للحقية فيه من سير نظر ولا تأمل في الدليل كان هذا المتبع جعل قول الغير أو فعله قلادة في عنقه وقيل : عبارة عن قول الغير بلا حجة ولا دليل .
(1) Taqlid: it is the following of a person of another in what he says or does, believing it to be true without any observation (nazar) or reflection on evidence, as if the follower makes the saying or action of the other a 'necklace' around his neck. And it is said: it is the saying of another without proof or evidence.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines taqlid etymologically and technically. Literally, it means 'to place a necklace' (qilada) around the neck, indicating blind acceptance. Technically, it is following another in speech or action, believing it true without personal nazar (observation) or tammul (reflection) on evidence. An alternative definition is simply accepting another's statement without proof or evidence (hujja or dalil). This definition is crucial for understanding the debate on whether taqlid suffices for iman.

Key Terms
تقليد
اتباع
نظر
تأمل
دليل
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think taqlid simply means learning from a teacher or following an authority in general.
Correction: The definition specifies that taqlid requires following without personal examination; it is not mere acceptance but blind acceptance lacking personal verification.

Checkpoint
What is the literal meaning of 'taqlid' as mentioned in the definition?
A) Acceptance based on evidence
B) The act of placing a necklace
C) Independent reasoning
D) Rejecting authority

Key Takeaway
Taqlid is following another without personal examination of evidence, both literally and technically defined as a form of blind acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah or His religion simply because you were raised with it, without ever personally examining the evidence from the Quran and Sunnah? What would it take for you to genuinely investigate that belief today, even if it feels uncomfortable? Are you willing to do that this week?
?
Think of a religious opinion you currently hold that you never really questioned. If it turns out to be based on taqlid—blind acceptance without evidence—would you be willing to change it? What is stopping you from looking into the evidence right now?
?
You may follow a particular scholar or school of thought. In which areas of your faith have you simply 'put their opinion around your neck like a necklace' without any personal reflection? Write down one specific area and commit to studying the evidence for it before this month ends.

Mu'tazili Views on Taqlid for the Incapable
Reports Mu'tazili views on taqlid for the incapable and introduces ashab al-jumal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili position that certain incapable individuals (common people, slaves, women) are required to follow correct opinion rather than engage in full reflection.

Core Idea
For the incapable, correct taqlid of truth is a valid obligation.

The Mu'tazila engaged deeply with the issue of who is required to perform rational reflection (nazar). Al-Ka'bi, Ibn Abi 'Ayyash, and others held that those unable to examine proofs are not excused from belief but are charged with following the truth-giver and correct opinion. Later Mu'tazila refined this to include listening to initial proofs, creating a category called 'ashab al-jumal' for those who understand the gist.

Guided Path
1
Read the report of al-Ka'bi's view
Carefully read the passage stating that rational ones among those charged with reflection are the masters of reflection, while others are charged with taqlid.
Note the terminology: 'arbab al-nazar' vs. 'al-'awamm'.
2
Understand the later Mu'tazili refinement
The later view adds that the incapable are charged only with listening to initial proofs; if they understand, they become ashab al-jumal.
If they cannot grasp even that, they are not obligated at all.
3
Identify who is considered obligated
List the categories: rational ones must reflect; common people, slaves, women must follow correct opinion; the incapable may be excused if they cannot grasp basic proofs.
This shows a spectrum of obligation based on ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه حكي عن الكعبي وابن أبي عياش و جمع آخر من المعتزلة أن العقلاء من من كلف النظر، وهم أرباب النظر، كلف التقليد والظن وهم العوام ، والعبيد، وكثير من النسوان لعجزهم عن النظر في الأدلة وتمييزها عن الشبه. لكنهم كلفوا تقليد المحق دون المبطل . والظن الصائب دون الخطأ . وذكر بعض المتأخرين منهم أن العاجزين كلفوا أن يسمعوا أوائل الدلائل التي تتسارع إلى الإفهام . فإن فهموا كفاهم وهم أصحاب الجمل، ولا يكلفون تلخيص العبارة، وإن لم يمكنهم الوقوف عليها، فليسوا مكلفين أصلا، وإنما خلقوا لانتفاع المكلفين بهم في الدنيا، وهم كثير من العوام ، والعبيد، والنسوان وصاحب الجمل عند المتكلمين هو الذي يعتقد الجمل التي اتفق عليها أهل الملة . ولا يدخل في الاختلافات بل يعتقد أن ما وافق منها تلك الجمل فحق . وما خالفهما فباطل وتلك الجمل هي أن الله تعالى واحد لا شريك له ولا مثل ، وأنه لم يزل قبل الزمان والمكان والعرش، وكل ما خلق، وأنه القديم، وما سواه محدث وأنه عدل في قضائه، صادق في إخباره لا يحب الفساد ولا يرضى لعباده الكفر، ولا يكلفهم ما لا يطيقونه، وأنه مصيب حكيم محسن في أفعاله وفي كل ما خلق وقضى وقدر، وأنه بعث الرسل، وأنزل الكتب ليتذكر من في سابق علمه أن يتذكر ويخشى ويلزم الحجة على من علم أنه لا يؤمن ويأبى . وأن الرضاء بقضائه واجب والتسليم لأمره ،لازم، ما شاء كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن يضل من يشاء ويهدي من يشاء، لا كالإضلال الذي علم به الشيطان . إلى غير ذلك من العقائد الإسلامية . فإن قيل : أكثر أهل الإسلام آخذون بالتقليد قاصرون أو مقصرون في الاستدلال، ولم تزل الصحابة ومن بعدهم من الأئمة، والخلفاء، والعلماء، يكتفون منهم بذلك ويجرون عليهم أحكام المسلمين، فما وجه هذ الاختلاف؟ وذهاب كثير من العلماء والمجتهدين إلى أنه لا صحة لإيمان المقلدين . قلنا: ليس الخلاف في هؤلاء الذين نشأوا في ديار الإسلام من الأمصار والقرى والصحارى، وتواتر عندهم حال النبي عليه السلام) وما أوتي به من المعجزات، ولا في الذين يتفكرون في خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار فإنهم كلهم من أهل النظر والاستدلال بل فيمن نشأ على شاهق جبل مثلاً، ولم يتفكر في ملكوت السموات والأرض فأخبره إنسان بما يفترض عليه اعتقاده، فصدقه فيما أخبره بمجرد إخباره من غير تفكر وتدبر
Moreover, it is reported from al-Ka'bi, Ibn Abi 'Ayyash, and another group of the Mu'tazila that the rational ones among those who are charged with reflection - and they are the masters of reflection - are charged with blind following and opinion, namely the common people, the slaves, and many of the women, due to their inability to examine proofs and distinguish them from fallacies. However, they are charged with following the truth-giver, not the falsifier, and correct opinion, not error. And some of their later scholars mentioned that the incapable are charged to listen to the initial proofs that quickly lead to understanding. If they understand, that suffices them, and they are the masters of the summaries (ashab al-jumal); they are not charged with summarizing the expression. But if they cannot grasp them, then they are not charged at all; they were only created for the benefit of the obligated persons in this world, and they are many of the common people, the slaves, and the women. And the master of the summaries (sahib al-jumal) according to the theologians is the one who believes the summaries that the people of the religious community have agreed upon. He does not enter into disagreements, but rather believes that whatever agrees with those summaries is true, and whatever contradicts them is false. And those summaries are: that Allah is one, no partner for Him nor any likeness; that He has always existed before time, place, the Throne, and everything He created; that He is the Eternal, and other than Him is originated; that He is just in His decree, truthful in His information, does not love corruption, does not approve for His servants disbelief, and does not charge them with what they cannot bear; that He is correct, wise, benevolent in His actions and in everything He created, decreed, and determined; that He sent messengers and revealed books so that those in His prior knowledge would remember, fear, and establish the proof against those He knows will not believe and will refuse; that contentment with His decree is obligatory and submission to His command is necessary; whatever He wills is, and whatever He does not will is not; He leads astray whom He wills and guides whom He wills, not like the misguidance that Satan is known by; and so on of Islamic beliefs. For if it is said: Most of the people of Islam are adherents of blind following, deficient or negligent in reasoning, yet the Companions and after them the Imams, Caliphs, and scholars have always been satisfied with that from them and applied to them the rulings of Muslims; so what is the basis of this difference? And many scholars and mujtahids hold that the faith of blind followers is not valid? We say: The disagreement is not about those who grew up in the lands of Islam in cities, villages, and deserts, and the condition of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the miracles he brought have been recurrently transmitted to them, nor about those who reflect on the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of night and day, for all of them are people of reflection and reasoning. Rather, it is about one who grew up, for example, on a high mountain, and did not reflect on the dominion of the heavens and the earth, and a person informed him of what he is supposed to believe, and he believed him in what he told him merely on the basis of his information without reflection and deliberation.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a report and discussion of various theological views on the obligation of reflection (nazar) for different classes of people, followed by a definition of the essential beliefs (jumal) and an objection-and-answer about the status of most Muslims. The author first reports from al-Ka'bi, Ibn Abi 'Ayyash, and other Mu'tazila that the rational ones (who are capable of reasoning) are required to reflect, while the common people, slaves, and many women are required to engage in blind following (taqlid) of correct opinion due to their inability to examine proofs. However, they must follow the truth-giver and correct opinion. Later Mu'tazila added that the incapable are required only to listen to basic proofs that quickly lead to understanding; if they understand, they are the 'ashab al-jumal' (masters of summary beliefs) and are not required to articulate proofs. If they cannot grasp even that, they are not obligated at all, created only for worldly benefit. The author then defines 'sahib al-jumal' as one who believes the agreed-upon summary doctrines of the Muslim community and does not delve into disagreements. He lists these essential beliefs: tawhid (oneness of Allah), His eternity and creation of all things, His justice, truthfulness, mercy, sending of messengers, revelation of books, predestination, contentment with His decree, and submission. Next, an objection is raised: most Muslims are muqallids (blind followers), yet the early community (companions, imams, caliphs, scholars) accepted them as believers and applied Islamic rulings to them; how can it then be said that the faith of muqallids is invalid? The response clarifies that the disagreement does not concern Muslims who grew up in Islamic lands where the Prophet's miracles are well-known and who reflect naturally on creation, for these are effectively people of reflection. Rather, the dispute concerns someone who grew up in isolation (e.g., on a mountain) who accepts belief purely based on another's word without any reflection. Thus, the difference is only about those who are capable of reflection but neglect it, not about the common person who arrives at belief through implicit reasoning or a general reflection on the signs of creation.

Key Terms
التقليد (taqlid)
النظر (nazar)
العوام (al-'awamm)
أصحاب الجمل (ashab al-jumal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazili scholars reported in this passage, what is the obligation of the incapable (common people, slaves, women)?
A) They must engage in full rational reflection like the masters of reflection.
B) They are excused from any belief obligation.
C) They must follow the truth-giver and correct opinion.
D) They must memorize the theological proofs.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila held that some individuals are not required to perform full reflection but must still follow correct belief through taqlid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a position of weakness or inability, do you actively seek out a truth-giver to follow, or do you passively accept whatever opinion is easiest? What does this passage say about your responsibility to discern the truth even when you cannot reason to it yourself?
?
If the Mu'tazila say that some people exist only for the benefit of others, how does that challenge your view of human purpose? Are you content to be a means for others' benefit, or do you desire the honor of being one of the obligated?
?
This passage shows that even the incapable must hold correct belief. Have you ever excused your own ignorance by claiming it is 'too difficult' to learn? What initial proofs have you heard, and have you truly listened to them?

The Essential Beliefs (Jumal) According to the Theologians
Defines sahib al-jumal and enumerates the essential consensus beliefs of Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'sahib al-jumal' and the core summary beliefs that all Muslims agree upon.

Core Idea
The agreed-upon summary beliefs define the boundaries of correct faith.

After introducing the concept of 'ashab al-jumal' for those who grasp the basics, the author defines who is considered a 'sahib al-jumal' and lists the essential doctrines that form the consensus of the Muslim community. These are the fundamental beliefs that even a layperson must affirm.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of sahib al-jumal
Understand that a sahib al-jumal is one who believes the summary doctrines agreed upon by the community and does not enter into disputes.
He accepts whatever agrees with these summaries and rejects what contradicts them.
2
Identify the list of essential beliefs
Go through the enumerated beliefs: tawhid, eternity, justice, prophethood, predestination, etc.
Note how the list reflects both Sunni and Mu'tazili themes.
3
Reflect on the role of these summaries
Consider how these beliefs function as the minimum requirement for a valid faith without delving into theological debates.
This allows the common person to have correct belief without scholarly expertise.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه حكي عن الكعبي وابن أبي عياش و جمع آخر من المعتزلة أن العقلاء من من كلف النظر، وهم أرباب النظر، كلف التقليد والظن وهم العوام ، والعبيد، وكثير من النسوان لعجزهم عن النظر في الأدلة وتمييزها عن الشبه. لكنهم كلفوا تقليد المحق دون المبطل . والظن الصائب دون الخطأ . وذكر بعض المتأخرين منهم أن العاجزين كلفوا أن يسمعوا أوائل الدلائل التي تتسارع إلى الإفهام . فإن فهموا كفاهم وهم أصحاب الجمل، ولا يكلفون تلخيص العبارة، وإن لم يمكنهم الوقوف عليها، فليسوا مكلفين أصلا، وإنما خلقوا لانتفاع المكلفين بهم في الدنيا، وهم كثير من العوام ، والعبيد، والنسوان وصاحب الجمل عند المتكلمين هو الذي يعتقد الجمل التي اتفق عليها أهل الملة . ولا يدخل في الاختلافات بل يعتقد أن ما وافق منها تلك الجمل فحق . وما خالفهما فباطل وتلك الجمل هي أن الله تعالى واحد لا شريك له ولا مثل ، وأنه لم يزل قبل الزمان والمكان والعرش، وكل ما خلق، وأنه القديم، وما سواه محدث وأنه عدل في قضائه، صادق في إخباره لا يحب الفساد ولا يرضى لعباده الكفر، ولا يكلفهم ما لا يطيقونه، وأنه مصيب حكيم محسن في أفعاله وفي كل ما خلق وقضى وقدر، وأنه بعث الرسل، وأنزل الكتب ليتذكر من في سابق علمه أن يتذكر ويخشى ويلزم الحجة على من علم أنه لا يؤمن ويأبى . وأن الرضاء بقضائه واجب والتسليم لأمره ،لازم، ما شاء كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن يضل من يشاء ويهدي من يشاء، لا كالإضلال الذي علم به الشيطان . إلى غير ذلك من العقائد الإسلامية . فإن قيل : أكثر أهل الإسلام آخذون بالتقليد قاصرون أو مقصرون في الاستدلال، ولم تزل الصحابة ومن بعدهم من الأئمة، والخلفاء، والعلماء، يكتفون منهم بذلك ويجرون عليهم أحكام المسلمين، فما وجه هذ الاختلاف؟ وذهاب كثير من العلماء والمجتهدين إلى أنه لا صحة لإيمان المقلدين . قلنا: ليس الخلاف في هؤلاء الذين نشأوا في ديار الإسلام من الأمصار والقرى والصحارى، وتواتر عندهم حال النبي عليه السلام) وما أوتي به من المعجزات، ولا في الذين يتفكرون في خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار فإنهم كلهم من أهل النظر والاستدلال بل فيمن نشأ على شاهق جبل مثلاً، ولم يتفكر في ملكوت السموات والأرض فأخبره إنسان بما يفترض عليه اعتقاده، فصدقه فيما أخبره بمجرد إخباره من غير تفكر وتدبر
Moreover, it is reported from al-Ka'bi, Ibn Abi 'Ayyash, and another group of the Mu'tazila that the rational ones among those who are charged with reflection - and they are the masters of reflection - are charged with blind following and opinion, namely the common people, the slaves, and many of the women, due to their inability to examine proofs and distinguish them from fallacies. However, they are charged with following the truth-giver, not the falsifier, and correct opinion, not error. And some of their later scholars mentioned that the incapable are charged to listen to the initial proofs that quickly lead to understanding. If they understand, that suffices them, and they are the masters of the summaries (ashab al-jumal); they are not charged with summarizing the expression. But if they cannot grasp them, then they are not charged at all; they were only created for the benefit of the obligated persons in this world, and they are many of the common people, the slaves, and the women. And the master of the summaries (sahib al-jumal) according to the theologians is the one who believes the summaries that the people of the religious community have agreed upon. He does not enter into disagreements, but rather believes that whatever agrees with those summaries is true, and whatever contradicts them is false. And those summaries are: that Allah is one, no partner for Him nor any likeness; that He has always existed before time, place, the Throne, and everything He created; that He is the Eternal, and other than Him is originated; that He is just in His decree, truthful in His information, does not love corruption, does not approve for His servants disbelief, and does not charge them with what they cannot bear; that He is correct, wise, benevolent in His actions and in everything He created, decreed, and determined; that He sent messengers and revealed books so that those in His prior knowledge would remember, fear, and establish the proof against those He knows will not believe and will refuse; that contentment with His decree is obligatory and submission to His command is necessary; whatever He wills is, and whatever He does not will is not; He leads astray whom He wills and guides whom He wills, not like the misguidance that Satan is known by; and so on of Islamic beliefs. For if it is said: Most of the people of Islam are adherents of blind following, deficient or negligent in reasoning, yet the Companions and after them the Imams, Caliphs, and scholars have always been satisfied with that from them and applied to them the rulings of Muslims; so what is the basis of this difference? And many scholars and mujtahids hold that the faith of blind followers is not valid? We say: The disagreement is not about those who grew up in the lands of Islam in cities, villages, and deserts, and the condition of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the miracles he brought have been recurrently transmitted to them, nor about those who reflect on the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of night and day, for all of them are people of reflection and reasoning. Rather, it is about one who grew up, for example, on a high mountain, and did not reflect on the dominion of the heavens and the earth, and a person informed him of what he is supposed to believe, and he believed him in what he told him merely on the basis of his information without reflection and deliberation.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a report and discussion of various theological views on the obligation of reflection (nazar) for different classes of people, followed by a definition of the essential beliefs (jumal) and an objection-and-answer about the status of most Muslims. The author first reports from al-Ka'bi, Ibn Abi 'Ayyash, and other Mu'tazila that the rational ones (who are capable of reasoning) are required to reflect, while the common people, slaves, and many women are required to engage in blind following (taqlid) of correct opinion due to their inability to examine proofs. However, they must follow the truth-giver and correct opinion. Later Mu'tazila added that the incapable are required only to listen to basic proofs that quickly lead to understanding; if they understand, they are the 'ashab al-jumal' (masters of summary beliefs) and are not required to articulate proofs. If they cannot grasp even that, they are not obligated at all, created only for worldly benefit. The author then defines 'sahib al-jumal' as one who believes the agreed-upon summary doctrines of the Muslim community and does not delve into disagreements. He lists these essential beliefs: tawhid (oneness of Allah), His eternity and creation of all things, His justice, truthfulness, mercy, sending of messengers, revelation of books, predestination, contentment with His decree, and submission. Next, an objection is raised: most Muslims are muqallids (blind followers), yet the early community (companions, imams, caliphs, scholars) accepted them as believers and applied Islamic rulings to them; how can it then be said that the faith of muqallids is invalid? The response clarifies that the disagreement does not concern Muslims who grew up in Islamic lands where the Prophet's miracles are well-known and who reflect naturally on creation, for these are effectively people of reflection. Rather, the dispute concerns someone who grew up in isolation (e.g., on a mountain) who accepts belief purely based on another's word without any reflection. Thus, the difference is only about those who are capable of reflection but neglect it, not about the common person who arrives at belief through implicit reasoning or a general reflection on the signs of creation.

Key Terms
أصحاب الجمل (ashab al-jumal)
العقائد الإسلامية (Islamic beliefs)
الملة (religious community)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for determining truth or falsehood according to the sahib al-jumal?
A) Whatever agrees with the consensus of scholars.
B) Whatever agrees with the summary beliefs.
C) Whatever is transmitted in the Quran and hadith.
D) Whatever is rationally demonstrable.

Key Takeaway
The essential beliefs (jumal) provide the framework for correct faith, and a Muslim who holds them without entering into disputes is considered a valid believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself entering into disagreements about details of faith without first grounding yourself in the agreed-upon summaries? How can you ensure that your theological discussions are built on the foundation of the jumal?
?
The passage says Allah does not charge beyond capacity. Reflect on a time you felt burdened by a religious obligation. Does this truth assure you, or do you still struggle with feelings of inadequacy? How can you internalize this belief in your daily worship?
?
Contentment with Allah's decree is obligatory. Which decree of Allah in your life do you find hardest to accept? What would change in your heart if you truly submitted to His command in that matter, trusting His wisdom and benevolence?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

Objection and Answer: Are Most Muslims Muqallids?
Presents an objection and answer about the validity of muqallids' faith, distinguishing implicit reflection from pure taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the objection that most Muslims are muqallids and the distinction between those who implicitly reflect and those who accept belief without any reflection.

Core Idea
Most Muslims in Islamic lands are effectively people of reflection, not mere muqallids.

A potential challenge to the views on taqlid is that the early Muslim community accepted the faith of ordinary people without requiring formal reasoning. The author provides a nuanced answer: the disagreement about muqallids is not about those who grew up in an Islamic environment with widespread knowledge of the Prophet's miracles and who reflect on creation, but about isolated individuals who accept belief purely on another's word without any thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about the early community's practice
Notice the claim that most Muslims are muqallids yet the early community accepted them.
This sets up the problem: if muqallids' faith is invalid, how were they treated as believers?
2
Understand the author's response distinguishing two groups
The author distinguishes between those in Islamic lands with tawatur and reflection, and isolated individuals.
The first group is effectively people of reflection, not true muqallids.
3
Identify who is truly a muqallid
Only those who accept belief purely based on another's report without any reflection are the subject of disagreement.
Their faith's validity is questionable.

Misconception & Correction

A) All Muslims who do not formally study theology.

Faith and Disbelief

Definition of Kufr and Scholarly Views
Defining kufr as absence of faith and examining definitions by al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's definition of kufr as the absence of faith, how it differs from takdhib, and the strengths and weaknesses of alternative definitions by al-Qadi and the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Kufr is broader than denial of truth; it is the absence of faith.

This passage presents the central definition of kufr in Islamic theology. The author first defines kufr as the absence of faith in one capable of it, making it more comprehensive than mere denial. Then he examines two alternative definitions: al-Qadi's reduction of kufr to denial of Allah, and the Mu'tazila's equation of kufr with evil deeds or failure of obligation. Each is assessed and found lacking, highlighting the precision needed in theological terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's definition
Begin by reading the author's definition: 'kufr is the absence of faith from one who is capable of it.' Notice that he immediately contrasts it with takdhib, saying kufr is more general.
This definition includes cases of ignorance, not just active denial.
2
Examine al-Qadi's view
Next, consider al-Qadi's definition: kufr as jahl (denial/ignorance) of Allah. The passage raises an objection: a disbeliever may know Allah, and a believer may be ignorant of some rulings.
This objection challenges the equation of kufr with ignorance.
3
Understand the defense of al-Qadi
The response clarifies that 'jahl' here means denial of what is known definitively from the religion, or ignorance of it in general or detail. Reflect on how this defense narrows al-Qadi's definition.
This shows the importance of qualifying terms in theological definitions.
4
Consider the Mu'tazila view
Finally, read the Mu'tazila definition: kufr as an evil deed or failure of obligation deserving the greatest punishment. Note the author's criticism: 'there is manifest obscurity in this.'
Think about why this definition is problematic: it conflates belief with action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المبحث السادس - الكفر عدم الإيمان عما من شأنه. وهو أعم من التكذيب لشموله الكافر الخالي عن التصديق والتكذيب، وقال القاضي: هو الجحد بالله، وفسر بالجهل، ورد بأن الكافر قد يعرف الله ويصدق به، والمؤمن قد لا يعرف بعض أحكامه، فأجيب بأن المراد الجحد به في شيء مما علم قطعاً أنه من أحكامه، أو الجهل بذلك إجمالاً وتفصيلاً، وقالت المعتزلة: هو قبيح أو إخلال بواجب يستحق به أعظم العقاب، وفيه خفاء ظاهر.
He said: The sixth topic - Kufr is the absence of faith from one who is capable of it. And it is more general than takdhib (denial) because it includes the disbeliever who is devoid of both tasdiq (affirmation) and takdhib. Al-Qadi said: It is denial (jahl) of Allah, and he interpreted it as ignorance (jahl). But it was refuted that the disbeliever may know Allah and affirm Him, and the believer may not know some of His rulings. So it was answered: What is meant is denial of Him in something that is known definitively to be from His rulings, or ignorance of that in general or in detail. The Mu'tazila said: It is an evil (qabih) or a failure to perform an obligation that deserves the greatest punishment, and there is manifest obscurity in this.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage defines kufr and presents three views. The author's definition: 'kufr is the absence of faith from one who is capable of it' (adam al-iman 'amma min sha'nihi). He explains that this is broader than takdhib (denial) because it includes a person who lacks both tasdiq (affirmation) and takdhib (denial) — i.e., a purely ignorant person. Then al-Qadi's definition: kufr is jahl (denial) of Allah, interpreted as ignorance (jahl). This is refuted because a disbeliever may know Allah and affirm Him, and a believer may be ignorant of some rulings. The response is that jahl here means denial of what is necessarily known from the religion, or ignorance of it. Finally, the Mu'tazila define kufr as an evil deed (qabih) or failure to perform an obligation that merits the greatest punishment. The author notes this has manifest obscurity.

Key Terms
كفر
تكذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kufr is the same as takdhib (active denial).
Correction: Kufr is broader: it includes the absence of faith even without explicit denial, such as ignorance or indifference.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is the definition of kufr?
A) Denial (jahl) of Allah.
B) Evil deed or neglect of an obligation.
C) Absence of faith from one who is capable of it.
D) Disbelief in the prophets.

Key Takeaway
Kufr is primarily the absence of faith in one capable of it, not merely active denial or sinful action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that simply not denying Allah or Islam is enough for faith? This lesson shows that the absence of affirmation is itself kufr. Where in your life have you been ‘devoid of both affirmation and denial’ towards Allah’s commands? What one area will you actively affirm today?
?
Al-Qadi’s definition highlights ignorance of necessarily known matters as kufr. Are there truths about Allah, the Prophet, or the religion that you are aware of but fail to internalize or act upon? How does this lesson make you accountable for turning passive knowledge into active iman?
?
The Mu’tazila defined kufr as an evil deed or failure of obligation deserving punishment. Though the author finds obscurity in that, it forces you to ask: Do you treat kufr as a distant label for others, or do you feel the weight of the very absence of faith in your own heart? What concrete step will you take today to ensure your iman is not just present but deeply felt?

Acts as Signs of Disbelief: The Lawgiver's Designation
Answering the objection that actions can cause kufr while tasdiq remains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians answer the objection that a person can commit acts of disbelief while still affirming faith internally.

Core Idea
Not all prohibited acts are signs of denial; only those designated by the Lawgiver.

In this passage, our author addresses a key objection to the definition of disbelief: if disbelief is fundamentally the denial of the heart, how can external actions cause disbelief? The response introduces the concept of acts being 'signs' of denial, a designation that belongs solely to the Lawgiver. This clarifies that not every sin expels one from faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: objection (فإن قيل) then answer (قلنا).
2
Analyze the concessive answer
The author says 'لو سلم' — even if we grant your premise. This is a dialectical move. Understand why he concedes the point.
Concession does not mean acceptance; it sets up a different explanation.
3
Identify the key concept: 'sign of takdhib'
The answer hinges on the idea that some prohibitions are designated as signs (علامة) of denial. Find this in the Arabic and note the phrase 'وذلك إلى الشارع' (that is up to the Lawgiver).
This limits which acts cause kufr.
4
Examine the references to al-Ghazali and al-Razi
Al-Ghazali's view includes the disbeliever devoid of both tasdiq and takdhib. Al-Razi's objection is deemed weak because the prohibition against blanket equation is clear.
This shows how the answer defends the definition from alternative interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: قد يكفر المكلف بعض أفعاله مع أن تصديقه بحاله. قلنا لو سلم، فيجوز أن يكون بعض المحظورات علامة التكذيب دون البعض، وذلك إلى الشارع، وكذا بعض التأويلات في الأصول. وهذا معنى عدم تصديق النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم في شيء مما علم مجيئه به على ما ذكره الإمام الغزالي لشموله الكافر الخالي عن التصديق والتكذيب، واعتبر الإمام الرازي بأن من جملة ما جاء به النبي أن تصديقه واجب في كل ما جاء به، فمن لم يصدقه فقد كذبه في ذلك ضعيف، لظهور المنع.
For it might be said: A morally responsible person may become a disbeliever through some of his actions while his affirmation (tasdiq) remains as it was. We say: Even if we grant that, then it is possible that some prohibitions are a sign (alama) of takdhib and not others, and that is up to the Lawgiver, and likewise some interpretations (ta'wilat) in the principles. And this is the meaning of not affirming the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) in something that is known to have come from him, according to what Imam al-Ghazali mentioned, because it includes the disbeliever who is devoid of both tasdiq and takdhib. Imam al-Razi considered that among what the Prophet brought is that affirming him is obligatory in everything he brought, so whoever does not affirm him has denied him in that, but this is weak, because the prohibition is clear.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer. The objection states that a person may commit acts of kufr while still having tasdiq (affirmation of faith). The answer begins by granting the premise (لو سلم) then explains that it is possible that some prohibitions are designated by the Lawgiver (al-shari') as signs (alama) of takdhib, while others are not. This is the meaning of not tasdiq of the Prophet in something known to be from him, as al-Ghazali mentioned, including the case of a disbeliever who has neither tasdiq nor takdhib. Al-Razi's view that tasdiq is obligatory in everything the Prophet brought, so that failure to affirm is takdhib, is deemed weak (da'if) because of the clear prohibition of such a blanket equation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sin or prohibited act automatically makes a person a disbeliever.
Correction: Only those acts that the Lawgiver has specifically designated as signs of takdhib constitute disbelief. Other prohibitions do not remove one from faith.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the objection, what determines whether an act is a sign of takdhib?
A) The act's inherent nature.
B) The intention of the doer.
C) The designation by the Lawgiver (al-shari').
D) The consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Actions can cause disbelief only when the Lawgiver designates them as signs of denial, not by their own nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an action you habitually commit that you secretly fear might be a sign of denial? Have you ever sought to know whether the Lawgiver designated it as such, or have you simply dismissed it as trivial? What would change if you took the possibility seriously today?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim fall into a major sin, do you quickly assume their faith is compromised? How does this lesson force you to check your judgment against the Lawgiver's designation rather than your own feelings? Will you now ask Allah for guidance before labeling anyone?
?
Al-Razi's view that any failure to affirm the Prophet is takdhib was deemed weak. But in your own life, are there areas where you selectively obey the Prophet? What would it mean for you to treat every command of the Prophet as a potential sign of your affirmation or denial? When will you start?

Acts of Istikhfaf and the Lawgiver's Designation
Objection and answer about acts of istikhfaf and the universal definition of kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Lawgiver can designate certain prohibited acts as signs of takdhib, making the doer a kafir even if tasdiq remains.

Core Idea
The Lawgiver can make some prohibited acts signs of takdhib, overriding apparent tasdiq.

This lesson addresses a second objection to the definitions of kufr. The objector points to consensus cases where a person commits blasphemous acts yet affirms the truth of Islam. If kufr is defined simply as absence of tasdiq or as committing prohibited acts, these cases seem contradictory. The answer explains how the Lawgiver can designate specific acts as signs of takdhib, so that the person is judged as a disbeliever despite verbal affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the objection's examples and its two logical consequences.
The objection is from an imaginary opponent challenging the definitions.
2
Understand the two prongs of the objection
First, if kufr is defined as absence of tasdiq, then acts like istikhfaf would not be kufr because tasdiq is present. Second, if kufr is defined as committing prohibited acts, then all sinners (fussaq) would be kafir.
This shows the definitions fail both ways.
3
Study the answer and its key move
The answer grants coexistence of tasdiq with those acts, then asserts the Lawgiver can designate some prohibited acts as signs of takdhib. Thus the person is judged mukadhdhib and lacking tasdiq in a legal sense.
The answer does not deny the objection's premises but reinterprets them through divine designation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: من استخف بالشرع أو الشارع، أو ألقى المصحف في القاذورات، أو شد الزنار بالاختيار كافر إجماعاً، وإن كان مصدقاً للنبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم في جميع ما جاء به، وحينئذ يبطل عكس التعريفين، وإن جعلت ترك المأمور به أو ارتكاب المنهى عنه علامة التكذيب وعدم التصديق بطل طردهما بغير الكفرة من الفساق. قلنا: لوسلم اجتماع التصديق المعتبر في الإيمان مع تلك الأمور التي هي كفر وفاقاً، فيجوز أن يجعل الشارع بعض محظورات الشرع علامة التكذيب، فيحكم بكفر من ارتكبه وبوجود التكذيب فيه وانتفاء التصديق عنه، كالاستخفاف بالشرع وشد الزنار وبعضها، لا كالزنا وشرب الخمر، ويتفاوت ذلك إلى متفق عليه، ومختلف فيه ومنصوص عليه ومستنبط من الدليل، وتفاصيله في كتب الفروع.
For it might be said: Whoever treats the Shari'a or the Lawgiver lightly, or throws the mus'haf into filth, or deliberately ties the zunnar (waistband of a dhimmi) is a disbeliever by consensus, even if he is an affirmer (musaddiq) of the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) in all that he brought. In that case, the converse of the two definitions is invalidated; if one makes leaving what is commanded or committing what is forbidden a sign of takdhib and lack of tasdiq, then the definitions would not cover only the disbelievers but would include the sinful (fussaq) as well. We say: Even if we grant the coexistence of the tasdiq that is considered in faith with those acts that are kufr by agreement, then it is possible for the Lawgiver to make some prohibitions of the Shari'a a sign of takdhib, so that he who commits them is judged a disbeliever, with takdhib being present in him and tasdiq being absent from him, such as treating the Shari'a lightly and tying the zunnar and some others, not like fornication and drinking wine. And that varies into agreed upon, disputed, textually stated, and derived from evidence; their details are in the works of furu' (positive law).
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objection gives specific cases (istikhfaf of Shari'a or Lawgiver, throwing the mus'haf into filth, tying the zunnar) that are kufr by consensus even if the person is musaddiq. This invalidates the converse of the definitions of kufr (i.e., if kufr is defined as absence of tasdiq, then these cases would not be kufr because tasdiq is present; if kufr is defined as doing prohibited acts, then all sinners would be kafir). The answer again grants the coexistence of tasdiq with those acts, then asserts that the Lawgiver can designate some prohibited acts as signs of takdhib, so the person who commits them is judged as having takdhib and lacking tasdiq in the relevant sense. Such acts include istikhfaf and tying the zunnar, but not zina or drinking wine. The differentiation is based on agreement, dispute, textual proof, or derivation, detailed in furu' works.

المصحف
