فساق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any major sin makes one a kafir.
Correction: Only specific acts designated by the Lawgiver (like istikhfaf, tying zunnar, etc.) are signs of takdhib, not all major sins like fornication or drinking wine.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, how can a person be considered a kafir despite being musaddiq?
A) Because his actions nullify his faith by themselves.
B) Because the Lawgiver made some acts signs of takdhib, so he is judged as mukadhdhib.
C) Because he is ignorant of the rulings.
D) Because he has committed a major sin.

Key Takeaway
The Lawgiver can designate certain prohibited acts as signs of takdhib, making the doer a kafir even if he affirms inwardly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered a casual joke or dismissive attitude towards Islamic rulings to be harmless? What does this lesson teach you about the gravity of istikhfaf, and how will you guard your tongue and actions from tomorrow onward?
?
Do you rely on your inner belief (tasdiq) while neglecting outward signs of respect for the Shari'a? Take a moment to examine your daily actions: is there anything you do that could be seen as a sign of takdhib? Write down one change you will make today.
?
If the Lawgiver can designate specific acts as overriding your apparent faith, what does that mean for your excuses about minor sins? Are you willing to submit your entire conduct—not just your heart—to divine judgment? Commit to one act of reverence you will perform today as a concrete response.

The Takdhib Definition of Kufr
The author defines kufr as takdhib and refines it by scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the takdhib definition of kufr, its refinements regarding the mukallaf and the child, and the disagreement about the validity of a child's faith.

Core Idea
Kufr is denial of the Prophet, but only from someone obligated to believe.

The author begins by examining the definition of kufr as takdhib (denial) of the Prophet. He refines this definition by specifying who is subject to this judgment and addresses the case of an intelligent child. This lesson clarifies the core of the takdhib definition and its immediate qualifications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of literal interpretation
Read the first sentence: 'What is known with certainty from the religion that it is according to its apparent meaning, then its interpretation is a denial of the Prophet.' This sets the foundation for the takdhib definition.
This principle is crucial: allegorical interpretation of clear religious truths is tantamount to denial.
2
Identify the refinement to mukallaf
Notice that the author specifies 'from the responsible person (mukallaf)' to exclude the intelligent child. Consider why a child is not held accountable.
The child (sabi 'aqil) is not obligated to affirm, so he is not considered a kafir for lack of belief.
3
Analyze the two views on a child's faith
The passage mentions a disagreement: some hold a child's faith valid, others not. See how the definition adapts to each view.
This shows the flexibility of the definition: it is tied to whether the person is obligated to believe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما علم قطعاً من الدين أنه على ظاهره، فتأويله تكذيب للنبي، بخلاف البعض . ثم لا يخفى أن المراد التكذيب، أو عدم التصديق من المكلف ، ليخرج الصبي العاقل الذي لم يصدق أو صرح بالتكذيب. وأما عند القائلين بصحة إيمانه، وبأنه يكفر بصريح التكذيب وإن لم يكفر بترك التصديق، فالمراد التكذيب ممن يصح الإيمان وعدم التصديق ممن يجب عليه الإيمان ... وقال القاضي : الكفر هو الجحد بالله ، وربما يفسر الجحد بالجهل. واعترض بعدم انعكاسه فإن كثيراً من الكفرة عارفون بالله تعالی، مصدقون به (۱) غير جاحدين. وإداريد الجحد أو الجهل أعم من أن يكون بوجوده أو وحدانيته، أو شيء من صفاته وأفعاله وأحكامه، لزم تكفير كثير من أهل الإسلام المخالفين في الأصول لأن الحق واحد وفاقاً. وأجيب بأن المراد الجحد به في شيء مما علم قطعاً أنه من أحكامه أو الجهل بذلك إجمالاً وتفصيلاً، وحينئذ يطرد وينعكس . بل ربما يكون أحسن من التعريف بتكذيب النبي عليه السلام) أو عدم تصديقه . لشموله الكفر بالله من غير توسط النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم ككفر إبليس . وقالت المعتزلة : هو ارتكاب قبيح، أو إخلال بواجب يستحق به أعظم العقاب. ولا خفاء في أن هذا من أحكام الكفر، لا ذاتياته، ولا لوازمه البينة التي ينتقل الذهن منها إليه، ومع هذا فإن أريد أعظم العقاب على الإطلاق، لم يصدق إلا على ما هو أشد أنواع الكفر وإن أريد أعظم بالنسبة إلى ما دونه . صدق على كثير من المعاصي، وإن اريد بالنسبة إلى الفسق (۳) وقد فسروا الفسق بما يستحق به عقوبة دون عقوبة الكفر - فدور . أو بالخروج من طاعة الله بكبيرة ـ ومن الكبائر ما هو كفر - فلا يتناوله التعريف. وإن قيد الكبيرة بغير الكفر، عاد الدور. وبالجملة لا خفاء في اختلال هذا التعريف وخفائه.
What is known with certainty from the religion that it is according to its apparent meaning, then its interpretation is a denial of the Prophet, unlike some. Then it is not hidden that what is intended is denial, or lack of affirmation from the responsible person, to exclude the intelligent child who did not affirm or openly declared denial. As for those who hold the validity of his faith, and that he becomes a disbeliever by explicit denial even if he does not become a disbeliever by abandoning affirmation, then what is intended is denial from one whose faith is valid and lack of affirmation from one upon whom faith is obligatory. And al-Qadi said: Kufr is denial of God, and sometimes denial is interpreted as ignorance. It was objected that it does not hold conversely, for many disbelievers know God, affirm Him, not denying Him. And if denial or ignorance is more general than being about His existence, or His oneness, or any of His attributes, acts, and judgments, then it would necessitate declaring many Muslims who differ in fundamentals as disbelievers, for the truth is one unanimously. It was answered that what is meant is denial of Him in something that is known with certainty to be among His judgments, or ignorance of that in general and in detail; then it is coextensive and converse. Rather, it may be better than the definition by denial of the Prophet (peace be upon him) or lack of affirmation, because it includes disbelief in God without the mediation of the Prophet, such as the disbelief of Iblis. And the Mu'tazila said: It is committing an evil deed or violating an obligation that merits the greatest punishment. There is no doubt that this is among the consequences of kufr, not its essences, nor its clear necessary concomitants from which the mind moves to it. Moreover, if what is intended is the greatest punishment absolutely, it only applies to the most severe type of kufr; if what is intended is the greatest relative to what is below it, it applies to many sins; and if it is intended relative to fisq—and they defined fisq as what merits a punishment less than the punishment of kufr—then it is circular. Or [they define kufr as] leaving obedience to God by a major sin—and among major sins is what is kufr—so the definition does not include it. And if one restricts major sin to what is not kufr, circularity returns. In summary, there is no doubt about the defectiveness and obscurity of this definition.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an extended critical examination of various definitions of kufr (disbelief). The author first discusses the definition based on takdhib (denial or falsification) of the Prophet. He notes that something known with certainty from religion must be taken literally; interpreting it allegorically amounts to takdhib of the Prophet. Then he refines: the intended meaning of takdhib is either explicit denial or failure to affirm (tasdiq) by the responsible adult (mukallaf), excluding a child who has not yet affirmed or who explicitly denies. However, there is a disagreement: some hold that a child can have valid faith and become a kafir only by explicit denial. The author then presents the definition of al-Qadi (likely Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar or another): kufr is juhud (denial or rejection) of God, sometimes explained as ignorance. An objection is raised: this definition is not fully converse (i.e., it does not capture all instances), because many polytheists know God and affirm Him but are not deniers. Moreover, if juhud or jahl is taken broadly to include denial of existence, oneness, attributes, etc., it would wrongly make many Muslims kafir for differing in subsidiary matters, since truth is single. The answer is that juhud here refers to denial or ignorance of something known with certainty to be from God's judgments (ahkam), and in that case the definition is both coextensive and converse. In fact, it may even be superior to the takdhib definition because it covers disbelief in God without prophetic mediation, like Iblis's disbelief. Then the author critiques the Mu'tazila definition: kufr is committing a grave sin or omitting an obligation that merits the greatest punishment. He points out that this describes a consequence of kufr, not its essence or its necessary concomitants. If 'greatest punishment' means absolutely the severest, it only covers the worst kufr; if relative, it covers many ordinary sins. If relative to fisq (sin less than kufr), their own definition of fisq as meriting a lesser punishment makes the definition circular. If they define kufr as leaving God's obedience by a major sin, but some major sins are kufr, the definition fails to include those; if they restrict major sin to non-kufr, circularity returns. The author concludes that this definition is clearly defective and obscure.

Key Terms
كفر
تكذيب
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That kufr is simply denying the Prophet without any qualification.
Correction: The definition is refined: it applies only to a mukallaf, and there is nuance regarding children.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is excluded from the takdhib definition by specifying 'from the responsible person (mukallaf)'?
A) The child who has not yet believed
B) The child who openly denies
C) The adult who never thought about religion
D) The Prophet himself

Key Takeaway
Kufr as takdhib is defined as denial or failure to affirm by a responsible adult, with nuances for children.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any clear truths from the Quran and Sunnah that you have been tempted to interpret away to suit your desires? How does this lesson challenge you to stop rationalizing and submit fully?
?
As a responsible adult (mukallaf), do you take your obligation to affirm the truth seriously? Is there an area where you have remained silent or failed to affirm something you know is true, and what will you do about it now?
?
The passage shows a disagreement about the faith of children. Does this make you more cautious in judging the faith of others, especially those who may not have reached full accountability? How can you apply this humility in your interactions?

The Juhud Definition of Kufr and Its Defense
Al-Qadi defines kufr as juhud, objection and response, comparison with takdhib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi's definition of kufr as juhud (denial) of God, the objection that it is not converse, the response, and why it may be superior to the takdhib definition.

Core Idea
Kufr is denial of God, even without prophetic mediation, as in Iblis.

The author presents al-Qadi's definition of kufr as juhud (denial) of God, sometimes equated with ignorance. An objection is raised that many disbelievers know God and are not deniers. The response refines the definition to apply to denial of what is known with certainty from God's judgments. This lesson explores this definition and its advantages over the takdhib definition.

Guided Path
1
Grasp al-Qadi's definition and the objection
Read the definition: 'Kufr is denial of God.' Note the objection that it does not hold conversely because many disbelievers know God and affirm Him.
The objection is that the definition is not coextensive (laysa mun'akis).
2
Understand the response and refinement
The response restricts denial to something known with certainty from God's judgments, making the definition coextensive and converse.
This refinement avoids declaring Muslims who differ in subsidiary matters as kafir.
3
Compare with the takdhib definition
The author claims the juhud definition may be superior because it covers disbelief without prophetic mediation, like Iblis.
Consider why Iblis is not covered by takdhib definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما علم قطعاً من الدين أنه على ظاهره، فتأويله تكذيب للنبي، بخلاف البعض . ثم لا يخفى أن المراد التكذيب، أو عدم التصديق من المكلف ، ليخرج الصبي العاقل الذي لم يصدق أو صرح بالتكذيب. وأما عند القائلين بصحة إيمانه، وبأنه يكفر بصريح التكذيب وإن لم يكفر بترك التصديق، فالمراد التكذيب ممن يصح الإيمان وعدم التصديق ممن يجب عليه الإيمان ... وقال القاضي : الكفر هو الجحد بالله ، وربما يفسر الجحد بالجهل. واعترض بعدم انعكاسه فإن كثيراً من الكفرة عارفون بالله تعالی، مصدقون به (۱) غير جاحدين. وإداريد الجحد أو الجهل أعم من أن يكون بوجوده أو وحدانيته، أو شيء من صفاته وأفعاله وأحكامه، لزم تكفير كثير من أهل الإسلام المخالفين في الأصول لأن الحق واحد وفاقاً. وأجيب بأن المراد الجحد به في شيء مما علم قطعاً أنه من أحكامه أو الجهل بذلك إجمالاً وتفصيلاً، وحينئذ يطرد وينعكس . بل ربما يكون أحسن من التعريف بتكذيب النبي عليه السلام) أو عدم تصديقه . لشموله الكفر بالله من غير توسط النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم ككفر إبليس . وقالت المعتزلة : هو ارتكاب قبيح، أو إخلال بواجب يستحق به أعظم العقاب. ولا خفاء في أن هذا من أحكام الكفر، لا ذاتياته، ولا لوازمه البينة التي ينتقل الذهن منها إليه، ومع هذا فإن أريد أعظم العقاب على الإطلاق، لم يصدق إلا على ما هو أشد أنواع الكفر وإن أريد أعظم بالنسبة إلى ما دونه . صدق على كثير من المعاصي، وإن اريد بالنسبة إلى الفسق (۳) وقد فسروا الفسق بما يستحق به عقوبة دون عقوبة الكفر - فدور . أو بالخروج من طاعة الله بكبيرة ـ ومن الكبائر ما هو كفر - فلا يتناوله التعريف. وإن قيد الكبيرة بغير الكفر، عاد الدور. وبالجملة لا خفاء في اختلال هذا التعريف وخفائه.
What is known with certainty from the religion that it is according to its apparent meaning, then its interpretation is a denial of the Prophet, unlike some. Then it is not hidden that what is intended is denial, or lack of affirmation from the responsible person, to exclude the intelligent child who did not affirm or openly declared denial. As for those who hold the validity of his faith, and that he becomes a disbeliever by explicit denial even if he does not become a disbeliever by abandoning affirmation, then what is intended is denial from one whose faith is valid and lack of affirmation from one upon whom faith is obligatory. And al-Qadi said: Kufr is denial of God, and sometimes denial is interpreted as ignorance. It was objected that it does not hold conversely, for many disbelievers know God, affirm Him, not denying Him. And if denial or ignorance is more general than being about His existence, or His oneness, or any of His attributes, acts, and judgments, then it would necessitate declaring many Muslims who differ in fundamentals as disbelievers, for the truth is one unanimously. It was answered that what is meant is denial of Him in something that is known with certainty to be among His judgments, or ignorance of that in general and in detail; then it is coextensive and converse. Rather, it may be better than the definition by denial of the Prophet (peace be upon him) or lack of affirmation, because it includes disbelief in God without the mediation of the Prophet, such as the disbelief of Iblis. And the Mu'tazila said: It is committing an evil deed or violating an obligation that merits the greatest punishment. There is no doubt that this is among the consequences of kufr, not its essences, nor its clear necessary concomitants from which the mind moves to it. Moreover, if what is intended is the greatest punishment absolutely, it only applies to the most severe type of kufr; if what is intended is the greatest relative to what is below it, it applies to many sins; and if it is intended relative to fisq—and they defined fisq as what merits a punishment less than the punishment of kufr—then it is circular. Or [they define kufr as] leaving obedience to God by a major sin—and among major sins is what is kufr—so the definition does not include it. And if one restricts major sin to what is not kufr, circularity returns. In summary, there is no doubt about the defectiveness and obscurity of this definition.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an extended critical examination of various definitions of kufr (disbelief). The author first discusses the definition based on takdhib (denial or falsification) of the Prophet. He notes that something known with certainty from religion must be taken literally; interpreting it allegorically amounts to takdhib of the Prophet. Then he refines: the intended meaning of takdhib is either explicit denial or failure to affirm (tasdiq) by the responsible adult (mukallaf), excluding a child who has not yet affirmed or who explicitly denies. However, there is a disagreement: some hold that a child can have valid faith and become a kafir only by explicit denial. The author then presents the definition of al-Qadi (likely Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar or another): kufr is juhud (denial or rejection) of God, sometimes explained as ignorance. An objection is raised: this definition is not fully converse (i.e., it does not capture all instances), because many polytheists know God and affirm Him but are not deniers. Moreover, if juhud or jahl is taken broadly to include denial of existence, oneness, attributes, etc., it would wrongly make many Muslims kafir for differing in subsidiary matters, since truth is single. The answer is that juhud here refers to denial or ignorance of something known with certainty to be from God's judgments (ahkam), and in that case the definition is both coextensive and converse. In fact, it may even be superior to the takdhib definition because it covers disbelief in God without prophetic mediation, like Iblis's disbelief. Then the author critiques the Mu'tazila definition: kufr is committing a grave sin or omitting an obligation that merits the greatest punishment. He points out that this describes a consequence of kufr, not its essence or its necessary concomitants. If 'greatest punishment' means absolutely the severest, it only covers the worst kufr; if relative, it covers many ordinary sins. If relative to fisq (sin less than kufr), their own definition of fisq as meriting a lesser punishment makes the definition circular. If they define kufr as leaving God's obedience by a major sin, but some major sins are kufr, the definition fails to include those; if they restrict major sin to non-kufr, circularity returns. The author concludes that this definition is clearly defective and obscure.

Key Terms
كفر
جحد
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the juhud definition automatically classifies all non-Muslims as deniers, even those who believe in God.
Correction: The definition is refined to apply to denial of what is known with certainty from God's judgments, not just any denial.

Checkpoint
What is the key refinement that makes al-Qadi's definition of kufr as juhud both coextensive and converse?
A) Including ignorance as a form of denial
B) Restricting juhud to denial of what is known with certainty from God's judgments
C) Requiring prophetic mediation
D) Excluding those who affirm God's existence

Key Takeaway
Kufr as juhud is denial of God's known judgments, covering even disbelief without a prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you known something to be a clear command of God but chose to ignore or rationalize it away? What was the cost of that denial?
?
Iblis's kufr stemmed from pride—he refused to prostrate because he thought himself better. In what areas of your life does pride prevent you from submitting to what you know is true?
?
This lesson distinguishes between knowing God and submitting to His judgments. Are there truths about God or His commands that you intellectually affirm but have not yet acted upon? What would it take to turn that knowledge into obedience?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
Critique of the Mu'tazila Definition of Kufr
The author critiques the Mu'tazila definition of kufr, highlighting its consequence-based nature and logical flaws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definition of kufr as committing a grave sin or omitting an obligation meriting the greatest punishment, and the author's critique that it is defective and circular.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila definition confuses the consequence of kufr with its essence.

The author turns to the Mu'tazila definition of kufr: committing a grave sin or violating an obligation that merits the greatest punishment. He argues that this describes a consequence of kufr, not its essence. Moreover, he points out logical problems: if 'greatest punishment' is taken absolutely, it only covers the worst type of kufr; if relatively, it covers many sins; if relative to their definition of fisq, it becomes circular. This lesson unpacks these objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila definition
Read: 'Kufr is committing an evil deed or violating an obligation that merits the greatest punishment.' Note that the author says this is a consequence, not an essence.
This is a key distinction: the definition does not capture what kufr is in itself.
2
Analyze the three interpretations of 'greatest punishment'
The author presents three possibilities: absolute, relative to lesser sins, relative to fisq. Trace each and see the problems.
The third interpretation leads to circularity because fisq is defined in terms of kufr's punishment.
3
Understand the circularity with major sin
If kufr is defined as leaving obedience by a major sin, but some major sins are kufr, the definition fails. If they restrict major sin to non-kufr, circularity returns.
This demonstrates the incoherence of the Mu'tazila definition.

?
If the greatest punishment is not the defining feature of kufr, what is? How does this lesson challenge you to re-evaluate your fear of punishment versus your love for Allah as the basis for avoiding kufr? What concrete change in your perspective will you make today?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Types of Kafir: Munafiq, Murtadd, Mushrik, Kitabi, Dahri, Mu'attil, Zindiq
The author enumerates seven specific labels for disbelievers with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific terms used in Islamic theology to classify different forms of disbelief (kufr) and the conditions that trigger each label.

Core Idea
Disbelief is not monolithic; specific terms distinguish each type based on nuanced criteria.

In this final section of the discourse on faith, the author provides a systematic categorization of disbelievers. He moves from the general term 'kafir' to more specific labels that apply when additional conditions are met. This classification helps clarify theological rulings and avoids confusion. Let's explore each category.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general term kafir
Read the opening line: 'الكافر اسم لمن لا إيمان له' – this establishes kafir as a general label for anyone without faith. Then read the entire passage to see how each specific term branches from this root.
Notice that the passage first lists the categories in a concise form, then expands on each.
2
Distinguish hypocrite and apostate
Identify the definitions of munafiq (hypocrite) and murtadd (apostate). The key difference is that munafiq outwardly shows faith while hiding disbelief, whereas murtadd was Muslim and then left Islam.
Both are subtypes of kafir but have different rulings in Islamic law.
3
Identify polytheists and scripturaries
Find the definitions of mushrik (polytheist) and kitabi (scriptuary). Mushrik affirms multiple gods; kitabi follows an abrogated scripture like Judaism or Christianity.
Kitabi is a specific category because they have a revealed book, even if abrogated.
4
Recognize materialists and deniers
Look for dahri (materialist) and mu'attil (denier). Dahri believes in the eternity of time and attributes events to it; mu'attil denies the existence of the Creator.
These two reject divine causality or existence outright.
5
Understand the hidden heretic
Examine the definition of zindiq: one who outwardly practices Islam and acknowledges the Prophet but secretly holds beliefs that are unanimously kufr. Also read the origin of the term from the book 'Zind'.
Zindiq is particularly dangerous because of deception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة - الكافر إن أظهر الإيمان خص باسم المنافق، وإن كفر بعد الإسلام فبالمرتد . وإن قال بتعدد الآلهة فالمشرك وإن تدين ببعض الأديان فبالكتابي، وإن أسند الحوادث إلى الزمان واعتقد قدمه فبالدهري . وإن نفى الصانع، فبالمعطل وإن أبطن عقائد هي كفر بالاتفاق فبالزنديق). قد ظهر أن الكافر اسم لمن لا إيمان له فإن أظهر الإيمان خص باسم المنافق، وإن طرأ كفره بعد الإسلام، خص باسم المرتد لرجوعه عن الإسلام، وإن قال بإلهين أو أكثر خص باسم المشرك لإثباته الشريك في الألوهية . وإن كان متديناً ببعض الأديان والكتب المنسوخة، خص باسم الكتابي كاليهودي والنصراني وإن كان يقول بقدم الدهر وإسناد الحوادث إليه خص باسم الدهري. وإن كان لا يثبت الباري تعالى خص باسم المعطل، وإن كان مع اعترافه بنبوة النبي (صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم وإظهاره شعائر الإسلام يبطن عقائد هي كفر بالاتفاق خص باسم الزنديق . وهو في الأصل منسوب إلى زند اسم كتاب أظهره مزدك في أيام قباد وزعم أنه تأويل كتاب المجوس الذي جاء به زرادشت الذي يزعمون أنه نبيهم.
He said: Conclusion – The disbeliever, if he displays faith, is specifically called a munafiq (hypocrite); and if he disbelieves after Islam, then a murtadd (apostate). And if he affirms multiple deities, then a mushrik (polytheist). And if he follows some religion, then a kitabi (Scriptuary). And if he ascribes events to time and believes in its eternity, then a dahri (materialist). And if he denies the Maker, then a mu'attil (denier of attributes). And if he conceals beliefs that are kufr by consensus, then a zindiq (heretic). It has become clear that the kafir is a name for one who has no faith. If he displays faith, he is specifically called munafiq; if his disbelief occurs after Islam, he is specifically called murtadd for his reversion from Islam; if he says there are two gods or more, he is specifically called mushrik for affirming a partner in divinity. And if he follows some of the abrogated religions and scriptures, he is specifically called kitabi, like the Jew and Christian. And if he holds the eternity of time and ascribes events to it, he is specifically called dahri. And if he does not affirm the Most High Creator, he is specifically called mu'attil. And if, despite his acknowledgment of the Prophet's prophethood (peace be upon him) and his display of Islamic rites, he conceals beliefs that are kufr by consensus, he is specifically called zindiq. It is originally attributed to Zind, a book brought forth by Muzdak in the days of Qubadh, claiming it was the interpretation of the Magian book brought by Zoroaster, whom they claim is their prophet.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a classification (khātima) where the author lists specific terms for different types of disbelievers based on their particular characteristics. He provides seven categories: (1) Munafiq – one who outwardly professes faith but inwardly disbelieves; (2) Murtadd – one who apostatizes after having been Muslim; (3) Mushrik – one who affirms multiple gods or associates partners with God; (4) Kitabi – one who adheres to a scriptural religion (Judaism or Christianity) whose scriptures are abrogated; (5) Dahri – one who believes in the eternity of time (dahr) and attributes events to it, denying divine intervention; (6) Mu'attil – one who denies the existence of the Creator; (7) Zindiq – one who outwardly displays Islam and acknowledges the Prophet but inwardly harbors beliefs that are unanimously kufr (e.g., secret heresy). The author notes that the origin of the term 'zindiq' is from the name of a book (Zind) produced by Muzdak during the reign of Qubadh, claiming to be an interpretation of the Zoroastrian scriptures. This classification shows that while 'kafir' is a general term, specific labels apply when additional conditions are met.

Key Terms
منافق
مرتد
مشرك
كتابي
دهري
معطل
زنديق
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'kafir' always requires explicit denial of faith; hidden disbelief or hypocrisy is not considered kufr.
Correction: The passage shows that munafiq (hypocrite) and zindiq (heretic) are also categories of kafir, even though they may outwardly appear Muslim or display faith.

Checkpoint
Which term is used for a person who outwardly practices Islam but secretly holds beliefs that are unanimously considered kufr?
A) Munafiq
B) Zindiq
C) Dahri
D) Mu'attil

Key Takeaway
Disbelief (kufr) has distinct categories defined by specific conditions, and the term used depends on the manner and circumstances of unbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever filtered your words or actions to appear more devout than you truly feel inside? Which category from this lesson does that tendency reflect, and what does it mean for your relationship with Allah? Commit to sincerity starting now.
?
When something bad happens, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'just bad luck' or 'that's the way things go' rather than seeing it as from Allah? How does the dahri's view challenge you, and what step will you take today to ascribe events to Allah alone?
?
If your heart were scanned tonight, would the beliefs you hold in private match the Islam you profess in public? Which of these seven categories would you most fear being placed in, and what is one specific hidden belief you must uproot?

When Not to Declare a Muslim a Kafir
The author begins the discussion on when a person of the Qibla is not to be declared a kafir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for takfir among Ahl al-Qibla and the different opinions on who is declared a disbeliever.

Core Idea
Not every error leads to disbelief; only denial of essential doctrines (daruriyyat al-din).

In this passage, the author addresses a sensitive topic: when can we declare a fellow Muslim a disbeliever? He sets the general rule that only contradicting necessary matters of religion warrants takfir, and then reports various opinions, including those of the early Mu'tazila. This lesson introduces the principle and highlights the diversity of views.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: a general principle, then alternative views.
The passage is incomplete; it breaks off mid-sentence.
2
Identify the author's ruling
Find the sentence that states the general rule: 'He is not a disbeliever unless he contradicts what is among the necessary matters of religion.'
3
Recognize the reported opinions
Note the opinions attributed to 'al-Ustadh' and the early Mu'tazila. What groups do they declare as disbelievers?
4
Understand 'daruriyyat al-din'
Reflect on what makes something a 'necessary matter of religion' (e.g., creation of the world, resurrection). Why are these considered essential?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ، المبحث السابع - في حكم مخلف الحق من أهل القبلة . ليس بكافر ما لم يخالف ما هو من ضروريات الدين ، كحدوث العالم، وحشر الأجساد وقيل : كافر وقال الاستاذ نكفر من أكفرنا، ومن لا ، فلا ، وقال قدماء المعتزلة نكفر المجبرة ، والقائلين بقدم الصفات، وخلق الأعمال، وجهلاء هم نكفر من قال بزيادة الصفات وبجواز الرؤية وبالخروج من النار، ويكون الشرور . والقبائح بخلقه وإرادته لنا أن النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) ومن بعده لم يكونوا يفتشون عن
He said: The seventh topic – on the ruling concerning one who differs from the truth among the people of the Qibla. He is not a disbeliever unless he contradicts what is among the necessary [matters] of religion, such as the temporal origination of the world and the resurrection of bodies. And it was said: [he is] a disbeliever. And al-Ustadh said: We declare disbeliever whoever declares us disbelievers, and whoever does not, we do not. And the early Mu'tazila said: We declare the Mujabbira and those who affirm the eternity of attributes and the creation of actions to be disbelievers, and their ignorant ones. We declare disbelievers those who affirm increase of attributes and the possibility of seeing [God] and exit from the Fire, and that evils and abominations are through His creation and will. It is [established] for us that the Prophet (peace be upon him) and those after him did not investigate about
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the 'seventh topic' (al-mabḥath al-sābiʿ) which discusses the ruling on a person of the Qibla (i.e., a Muslim who prays toward Mecca) who holds a wrong belief. The author states the general principle: such a person is not a disbeliever unless he contradicts something that is among the daruriyyat al-din (essential or necessarily known matters of religion), like the temporal creation of the world and the resurrection of bodies. He then notes that there is an alternative opinion (qīl) that such a person is a disbeliever. He then reports the view of 'al-Ustadh' (likely a reference to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini or similar) that we only declare as disbeliever those who declare us disbelievers; otherwise we do not. Then he reports the view of the early Mu'tazila, who declared certain groups as disbelievers: the Mujabbira (those who believe in absolute compulsion of human actions), those who affirm the eternity of God's attributes (as opposed to their being uncreated but not eternal in the sense of separate entities), those who affirm the creation of human actions by God, and among the ignorant of these groups. They also declared as disbelievers those who affirm an increase in God's attributes, the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya) in the afterlife, exit from the Fire (i.e., that sinners will eventually leave Hell), and that evil and abominations are created by God and willed by Him. The passage concludes with a reference to the Prophet and his companions, implying that they did not delve into such investigations, but the sentence is cut off. This lesson introduces the key principle that not every error leads to takfir, only denial of clear, necessary doctrines.

Misconception: Any theological disagreement leads to takfir.
Correction: The author restricts takfir to denial of daruriyyat al-din, and reports various opinions, showing that not every error warrants takfir.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the criterion for declaring a person of the Qibla a disbeliever?
A) If they deny any of the five pillars
B) If they contradict what is of the daruriyyat al-din (essential matters of religion)
C) If they follow a different madhhab
D) If they commit a major sin

Key Takeaway
A person of the Qibla is not declared a disbeliever unless they deny a necessary matter of religion (daruriyyat al-din).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The early Mu'tazila named specific groups as disbelievers. Is there any person or group you have silently categorized as ‘outside Islam’? Based on this lesson, what would it take for you to honestly reevaluate that verdict? Will you do it today?

Write down one relationship with a fellow Muslim you have distanced due to doctrinal differences, and commit to rebuilding it this week.

The General Term Kafir and Three Subcategories: Munafiq, Murtadd, Mushrik
Sanusi defines the general term 'kafir' and three subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general definition of kafir and the specific subcategories based on manifest behavior: munafiq (hypocrite), murtadd (apostate), and mushrik (polytheist).

Core Idea
Disbelief (kufr) is a broad term with precise subcategories based on manifest signs.

This passage from Sanusi's Sharh al-Maqasid establishes the foundational classification of disbelievers in Islamic theology. Before we can discuss rulings on different groups, we need a clear terminology. The author begins by defining the general term 'kafir' and then distinguishes three specific types based on outward actions and prior faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the definitions.
2
Identify the general term and its subcategories
Note that 'kafir' is the umbrella term, and 'munafiq', 'murtadd', 'mushrik' are specific types.
The first three are defined in the passage.
3
Understand the basis for each term
For each subcategory, ask: what condition triggers this label? Munafiq: shows faith outwardly. Murtadd: disbelieves after Islam. Mushrik: believes in multiple gods.
4
Reflect on the importance of precise terminology
Why is it important to distinguish these? Because the legal rulings and treatment differ for each.
This classification is used in fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة - الكافر إن أظهر الإيمان خص باسم المنافق، وإن كفر بعد الإسلام فبالمرتد. وإن قال بتعدد الآلهة فالمشرك، وإن تدين ببعض الأديان فبالكتابي، وإن أسند الحوادث إلى الزمان واعتقد قدمه فبالدهري. وإن نفى الصانع فبالمعطل، وإن أبطن عقائد هي كفر بالاتفاق فبالزنديق. قد ظهر أن الكافر اسم لمن لا إيمان له فإن أظهر الإيمان خص باسم المنافق، وإن طرأ كفره بعد الإسلام، خص باسم المرتد لرجوعه عن الإسلام، وإن قال بإلهين أو أكثر خص باسم المشرك لإثباته الشريك في الألوهية. وإن كان متديناً ببعض الأديان والكتب المنسوخة، خص باسم الكتابي كاليهودي والنصراني وإن كان يقول بقدم الدهر وإسناد الحوادث إليه خص باسم الدهري. وإن كان لا يثبت الباري تعالى خص باسم المعطل، وإن كان مع اعترافه بنبوة النبي (صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم) وإظهاره شعائر الإسلام يبطن عقائد هي كفر بالاتفاق خص باسم الزنديق. وهو في الأصل منسوب إلى زند اسم كتاب أظهره مزدك في أيام قباد وزعم أنه تأويل كتاب المجوس الذي جاء به زرادشت الذي يزعمون أنه نبيهم.
He said: Conclusion — The disbeliever, if he manifests faith, is specifically called a hypocrite (munafiq); and if he disbelieves after Islam, then (he is called) an apostate (murtadd). And if he says there are multiple gods, then (he is) a polytheist (mushrik). And if he adheres to some of the (previous) religions, then (he is) a Scriptuary (kitabi). And if he attributes events to time and believes in its pre-eternity, then (he is) a time-worshipper (dahri). And if he denies the Maker, then (he is) a negator (mu'attil). And if he conceals beliefs that are unanimously considered disbelief, then (he is) a heretic (zindiq). It has become clear that the disbeliever (kafir) is a term for one who has no faith (iman). Then if he manifests faith, he is specifically called a hypocrite (munafiq); and if his disbelief occurs after Islam, he is specifically called an apostate (murtadd) because of his turning back from Islam; and if he says there are two or more gods, he is specifically called a polytheist (mushrik) because of his affirming a partner in divinity; and if he adheres to some of the (previous) religions and abrogated scriptures, he is specifically called a Scriptuary (kitabi) like the Jew and the Christian; and if he speaks of the pre-eternity of time and attributes events to it, he is specifically called a time-worshipper (dahri); and if he does not affirm the Creator (al-Bari) exalted, he is specifically called a negator (mu'attil); and if, along with his acknowledgement of the prophethood of the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) and his manifesting the rites of Islam, he conceals beliefs that are unanimously considered disbelief, he is specifically called a heretic (zindiq). And it (the term zindiq) originally is attributed to Zand, which is the name of a book that Mazdak brought forth in the days of Qubadh, and he claimed that it was the interpretation of the book of the Magi which was brought by Zoroaster, whom they claim to be their prophet.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the various categories of disbeliever (kafir). The author begins by stating that the general term 'kafir' applies to anyone without faith (iman), and then specifies distinct subcategories based on particular conditions. The first distinction is based on manifest behavior: a disbeliever who outwardly shows faith is called a hypocrite (munafiq). If disbelief occurs after being a Muslim, he is called an apostate (murtadd). If he believes in multiple gods, he is a polytheist (mushrik). If he follows a previous revealed religion (like Judaism or Christianity), he is called a Scriptuary (kitabi). If he attributes events to time and believes time is eternal, he is a time-worshipper (dahri). If he denies the existence of the Creator, he is a negator (mu'attil). Finally, if he outwardly professes Islam but inwardly harbors beliefs unanimously deemed disbelief, he is a heretic (zindiq). The author then repeats the definitions in a more expanded form, emphasizing the reasoning behind each term. He also traces the origin of the term 'zindiq' to a book called 'Zand' produced by Mazdak, which was claimed to be an interpretation of the Zoroastrian scripture. This shows the historical context of the term. The passage is purely classificatory, establishing precise terminology for different types of disbelief, which is foundational for later rulings on takfir.

Key Terms
كافر
منافق
مرتد
مشرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kafir only refers to someone who openly rejects Islam.
Correction: Kafir includes those who outwardly show faith (munafiq) or who were Muslim and left (murtadd).

Checkpoint
Which term is used for a disbeliever who outwardly shows faith but conceals disbelief?
A) Kafir
B) Munafiq
C) Murtadd
D) Mushrik

Key Takeaway
The general term 'kafir' is specified by manifest behavior to yield distinct categories: munafiq, murtadd, mushrik.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were judged only by your outward actions, would anyone suspect you of being a munafiq? What hidden attitudes do you need to repent of today?
?
Have you ever considered that the line between kufr and iman can be crossed by a single belief or action? Which of your current habits might be edging toward shirk without you realizing it?
?
The murtadd leaves Islam openly—but are there areas of your life where you have silently 'apostatized' from obedience, acting as if Allah's commands no longer apply to you?

Further Categories of Disbelievers: Kitabi, Dahri, Mu'attil, Zindiq
Sanusi lists and explains four additional categories of disbelievers with historical etymology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the remaining categories of disbelievers: kitabi (Scriptuary), dahri (time-worshipper), mu'attil (negator), and zindiq (heretic), including the historical origin of 'zindiq'.

Core Idea
Disbelief takes many forms, from following abrogated scriptures to denying the Creator entirely.

After establishing the fundamental categories based on manifest behavior, the author now turns to broader classifications of disbelief. These include those who follow previous religions (kitabi), those who attribute events to time (dahri), those who deny Allah's existence (mu'attil), and those who secretly harbor disbelief while outwardly professing Islam (zindiq). The passage also provides the historical background of the term 'zindiq'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for lesson 2
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the four new categories.
2
Identify the four categories
Kitabi, dahri, mu'attil, zindiq. Notice their definitions and the reasons for their names.
3
Understand the unique feature of each
Kitabi: follows a previous scripture. Dahri: believes in eternal time. Mu'attil: denies the Creator. Zindiq: outwardly Muslim but inwardly heretical.
4
Learn the historical origin of 'zindiq'
The term comes from the book 'Zand' of Mazdak, showing its pre-Islamic usage.

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
The Ruling on Takfir Among Ahl al-Qibla
Author discusses the ruling on takfir of those who hold differing theological views within Ahl al-Qibla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical Sunni position that a person is not declared a disbeliever unless they deny a necessary doctrine of Islam, and the range of scholarly opinions on this issue.

Core Idea
Takfir is reserved for denial of necessary doctrines, not theological disagreement.

In this passage, the author confronts a delicate issue: when does theological disagreement cross into disbelief? He begins by stating the default ruling—no takfir without contradicting necessary doctrines—then surveys the views of major scholars and sects. This lesson unpacks that principle and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Read the opening statement: 'ليس بكافر ما لم يخالف ما هو من ضروريات الدين'. This is the core principle. Notice the examples given: temporality of the world and resurrection of bodies.
The author immediately gives the default position before presenting other opinions.
2
Survey the different opinions
The passage presents several views: al-Ash'ari and most companions, al-Shafi'i, Abu Hanifa, the early Mu'tazila, and the extremist position from al-Jubba'i. Compare the lenient vs. strict approaches.
Pay attention to the chain of takfir in al-Jubba'i's statement.
3
Understand the objection and response
The author addresses a potential objection: why didn't the Prophet investigate beliefs in detail? The response is that necessary matters were clear enough; general assent sufficed. This justifies focusing only on essential doctrines.
This argument supports the main principle by showing prophetic precedent.
4
Distinguish essential from disputed
The passage clarifies that the disagreement is about secondary issues like attributes, creation of actions, etc., while essential matters are agreed upon. Takfir only applies to denial of the essentials.
This distinction prevents misuse of takfir for sectarian disputes.

قال : المبحث السابع - في حكم مخلف الحق من أهل القبلة . ليس بكافر ما لم يخالف ما هو من ضروريات الدين ، كحدوث العالم، وحشر الأجساد وقيل : كافر وقال الاستاذ نكفر من أكفرنا، ومن لا ، فلا ، وقال قدماء المعتزلة نكفر المجبرة ، والقائلين بقدم الصفات، وخلق الأعمال، وجهلاء هم نكفر من قال بزيادة الصفات وبجواز الرؤية وبالخروج من النار، ويكون الشرور . والقبائح بخلقه وإرادته لنا أن النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) ومن بعده لم يكونوا يفتشون عن العقائد وينبهون على ما هو الحق . فإن قيل : فكذا في الأصول المتفق عليها . قلنا لاشتهارها، وظهور أدلتها على ما يليق بأصحاب الجمل. قد يقال : ترك البيان إنما كان اكتفاء بالتصديق الإجمالي، إذ التفصيل إنما يجب عند ملاحظة التفاصيل، وإلا فكم مؤمن لا يعرف معنى القديم والحادث هذا، وإكفار الفرق بعضها بعضاً مشهور . في باب الكفر والإيمان ومعناه أن الذين اتفقوا على ما هو من ضروريات الإسلام كحدوث العالم، وحشر الأجساد وما أشبه ذلك. واختلفوا في أصول سواها كمسألة الصفات وخلق الأعمال، وعموم الارادة ، وقدم الكلام ، وجواز الرؤية ، ونحو ذلك مما لا نزاع أن الحق فيها واحد هل يكفر المخالف للحق بذلك الاعتقاد وبالقول به أم لا؟ وإلا فلا نزاع في كفر أهل القبلة المواظب طول العمر على الطاعات باعتقاد قدم العالم، ونفي الحشر، ونفي العلم بالجزئيات، ونحو ذلك. وكذا بصدور شيء من موجبات الكفر عنه، أما الذي ذكرنا فذهب الشيخ الاشعري . وأكثر الأصحاب إلى أنه ليس بكافر، وبه يشعر ما قال الشافعي (رحمه الله تعالى : لا أرد شهادة أهل الأهواء إلا الخطابية ، لاستحلالهم الكذب، وفي المنتقى عن أبي حنيفة (رحمه الله تعالى أنه لم يكفر أحداً من أهل القبلة وعليه أكثر الفقهاء ومن أصحابنا من قال بكفر لمخالفين .وقالت قدماء المعتزلة بكفر القائلين بالصفات القديمة، وبخلق الأعمال، وكفر المجبرة ، حتى حكي عن الجبائي أنه قال : المجبر كافر. ومن شك في كفره فهو كافر ، ومن شك في كفر من شك في كفره فهو كافر. ومنهم من بلغ الغاية في الحماقة والوقاحة فزعم أن القول بزيادة الصفات ، وبجواز الرؤية، وبالخروج من النار، ويكون الشرور والقبائح بخلقه وإرادته ومشيئته ، وبجواز إظهار المعجزة على يد الكاذب كلها كفر.
He said: The seventh investigation — on the ruling of one who opposes the truth among the people of the qibla. He is not a disbeliever as long as he does not oppose what is among the necessities of the religion, such as the temporality of the world and the resurrection of bodies. And it was said: (he is) a disbeliever. And the master (al-Ustadh) said: We declare disbelievers those who declare us disbelievers, and whoever does not, then not. And the early Mu'tazila said: We declare disbelievers the Jabriyya, and those who affirm pre-eternal attributes, and the creation of actions, and their ignoramuses. They declared disbelievers those who said (affirm) increase of attributes, and the permissibility of seeing (God), and exiting from the Fire, and that evil and ugly things are by His creation and will. (They argued) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) and those after him did not investigate beliefs and point out what is true. For if it is said: So also in the agreed-upon principles. We say: Because of their fame and the clarity of their proofs in a manner befitting the followers of the summary. It may be said: The omission of explanation was only due to relying on general assent, for detail is only necessary when considering details. Otherwise, how many believers do not know the meaning of 'pre-eternal' and 'temporal'? And the mutual declaring of disbelief among sects is well-known. In the chapter of disbelief and faith, its meaning is that those who agree on what is among the necessities of Islam, like the temporality of the world and the resurrection of bodies and the like, and they disagree on other principles like the issue of attributes, creation of actions, universality of will, pre-eternity of speech, permissibility of seeing (God), and the like, concerning which there is no dispute that the truth is one — does the one who opposes the truth in that belief and statement become a disbeliever or not? Otherwise, there is no dispute regarding the disbelief of the people of the qibla who persist throughout life in acts of obedience while believing in the pre-eternity of the world, denial of resurrection, denial of knowledge of particulars, and the like. And likewise with the occurrence of anything that necessitates disbelief from him. As for what we mentioned, al-Ash'ari and most of the companions held that he is not a disbeliever. And it is indicated by what al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'I do not reject the testimony of the people of desires except the Khattabiyya, because of their permitting lies.' And in al-Muntada from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) that he did not declare any of the people of the qibla a disbeliever, and upon that are most of the jurists. And among our companions are those who said disbelief of the opponents. And the early Mu'tazila said the disbelief of those who affirm the pre-eternal attributes, and the creation of actions, and the disbelief of the Jabriyya, until it was related from al-Jubba'i that he said: 'The Jabri is a disbeliever. And whoever doubts his disbelief is a disbeliever, and whoever doubts the disbelief of the one who doubts his disbelief is a disbeliever.' And among them are those who reached the extreme in foolishness and shamelessness, who claimed that the saying (affirming) increase of attributes, and permissibility of seeing (God), and exiting from the Fire, and that evil and ugly things are by His creation, will, and volition, and permissibility of showing a miracle at the hand of a liar — all of it is disbelief.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the ruling on declaring someone a disbeliever (takfir) among those who pray towards the qibla (Ahl al-Qibla) but hold differing theological views. The author first states the default position: a person is not a disbeliever unless he contradicts what is necessarily known of the religion, like the createdness of the world and the resurrection of bodies. Then he presents various opinions. Al-Ustadh (perhaps al-Ash'ari) said we only declare disbelievers those who declare us disbelievers. The early Mu'tazila declared the Jabriyya (those who believe in compulsion) and others disbelievers. The author then mentions a potential objection: if the Prophet did not investigate beliefs in detail, why would we? The answer is that the necessary matters are so well-known that explicit explanation was unnecessary; reliance on general affirmation sufficed. The passage then elaborates that the disagreement is about non-essential matters (like attributes, creation of actions, etc.) while essential matters (like the createdness of the world) are agreed upon. The majority view, including al-Ash'ari, al-Shafi'i, and Abu Hanifa, is not to declare such opponents disbelievers. In contrast, some Mu'tazila, like al-Jubba'i, went to extremes, even declaring that anyone who doubts another's disbelief is a disbeliever. The passage ends by criticizing the extremists who consider many positions as disbelief. The overall argument is that takfir should be reserved for denial of necessary doctrines, not for disputed theological issues.

Key Terms
مخالف الحق
أهل القبلة
ضروريات الدين
تكفير
المجبرة
قدماء المعتزلة
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any theological disagreement justifies declaring another Muslim a disbeliever.
Correction: Classical Sunni scholars confined takfir to denial of necessary doctrines (ضروريات الدين) and warned against extremism in takfir.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the condition for not declaring a person a disbeliever among Ahl al-Qibla?
A) He must agree with al-Ash'ari on all issues.
B) He must not contradict what is necessarily known of the religion.
C) He must only follow the Prophet's Companions.
D) He must not use reason.

Key Takeaway
The default ruling is that a Muslim who affirms necessary doctrines is not declared a disbeliever due to disputed theological views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a fellow Muslim who holds a theological view you consider wrong, what is the first judgment that arises in your heart? Does that judgment reflect the caution of this lesson or the harshness it warns against?
?
This lesson teaches that the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not investigate every detail of belief with his companions. How does this uncover your own tendency to demand detailed agreement from others while perhaps ignoring the health of your own soul?
?
If you were to be judged for every theological error you hold that is not among the necessities of religion, what would your standing be before Allah? How does this thought move you to focus on correcting yourself before declaring others disbelievers?

Faith and Disbelief

The Framework of Excommunication: Essentials vs. Disputed Principles
Definition of the excommunication issue and the essential vs. secondary distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential Islamic doctrines (ḍarūriyyāt al-islām) and disputed principles (uṣūl siwāhā), and why disagreement on the latter does not automatically constitute disbelief.

Core Idea
Not all theological disagreements lead to excommunication.

This passage begins by setting the stage for the complex issue of excommunication (takfīr). It first clarifies that there is unanimous agreement on the fundamental essentials of Islam—anyone who denies these is undoubtedly a disbeliever. The real debate concerns secondary principles, where the truth is one but scholars differ on whether the opposite view is kufr. This lesson introduces that framing.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the issue
Read the first part of the Arabic passage carefully, noticing how it sets up the topic: 'In the chapter of disbelief and faith...'
Pay attention to the contrast between 'necessities of Islam' and 'other principles.'
2
Identify the two categories
The passage lists examples of essentials (originatedness of world, resurrection) and then secondary principles (attributes, creation of actions, etc.). See if you can spot the pivot: 'هل يكفر المخالف للحق' – the core question.
The answer to that question is the subject of the next lesson.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it prevents hasty takfīr and preserves unity within the Muslim community while acknowledging real theological differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في باب الكفر والإيمان ومعناه أن الذين اتفقوا على ما هو من ضروريات الإسلام كحدوث العالم، وحشر الأجساد وما أشبه ذلك. واختلفوا في أصول سواها كمسألة الصفات وخلق الأعمال، وعموم الارادة ، وقدم الكلام ، وجواز الرؤية ، ونحو ذلك مما لا نزاع أن الحق فيها واحد هل يكفر المخالف للحق بذلك الاعتقاد وبالقول به أم لا؟ وإلا فلا نزاع في كفر أهل القبلة المواظب طول العمر على الطاعات باعتقاد قدم العالم، ونفي الحشر، ونفي العلم بالجزئيات، ونحو ذلك. وكذا بصدور شيء من موجبات الكفر عنه، أما الذي ذكرنا فذهب الشيخ الاشعري . وأكثر الأصحاب إلى أنه ليس بكافر، وبه يشعر ما قال الشافعي (رحمه الله تعالى : لا أرد شهادة أهل الأهواء إلا الخطابية (١) ، لاستحلالهم الكذب، وفي المنتقى عن أبي حنيفة (رحمه الله تعالى أنه لم يكفر أحداً من أهل القبلة وعليه أكثر الفقهاء ومن أصحابنا من قال بكفر لمخالفين .وقالت قدماء المعتزلة بكفر القائلين بالصفات القديمة، وبخلق الأعمال، وكفر المجبرة ، حتى حكي عن الجبائي أنه قال : المجبر كافر. ومن شك في كفره فهو كافر ، ومن شك في كفر من شك في كفره فهو كافر. ومنهم من بلغ الغاية في الحماقة والوقاحة فزعم أن القول بزيادة الصفات ، وبجواز الرؤية، وبالخروج من النار، ويكون الشرور والقبائح بخلقه وإرادته ومشيئته ، وبجواز إظهار المعجزة على يد الكاذب كلها كفر.
In the chapter of disbelief (kufr) and faith (īmān): Its meaning is that those who agree upon what is among the necessities of Islam (ḍarūriyyāt al-islām) — such as the originatedness of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam), and the resurrection of bodies (ḥashr al-ajsād), and what resembles that — and they differ upon other principles (uṣūl siwāhā) such as the question of attributes, and the creation of actions, and the universality of will, and the pre-eternity of speech, and the permissibility of vision (of God), and the like of that, concerning which there is no dispute that the truth is one — does the one who opposes the truth by that belief and by speaking it become a disbeliever (kāfir) or not? Otherwise, there is no dispute about the disbelief of the people of the qibla who, while persisting throughout life in acts of obedience, come to believe in the pre-eternity of the world, and the denial of resurrection, and the denial of knowledge of particulars, and the like of that. And likewise regarding the occurrence of something from the causes of disbelief (mūjibāt al-kufr) from him. As for what we have mentioned, al-Shaykh al-Ashʿarī and most of the followers (al-aṣḥāb) went that he is not a disbeliever, and what al-Shāfiʿī (may God have mercy on him) said indicates it: 'I do not reject the testimony of the people of desires except the Khaṭṭābiyya, because they deem lying permissible.' And in al-Muntaqā from Abū Ḥanīfa (may God have mercy on him) that he did not excommunicate anyone from the people of the qibla, and upon that are most of the jurists. And among our companions are those who said the disbelief of the opponents. And the early Muʿtazila said the disbelief of those who affirm the eternal attributes (al-ṣifāt al-qadīma), and the creation of actions (khalq al-aʿmāl), and the disbelief of the Jabriyya (al-mujbira), so much so that it was narrated from al-Jubbāʾī that he said: 'The Jabari is a disbeliever. And whoever doubts his disbelief is a disbeliever, and whoever doubts the disbelief of the one who doubts his disbelief is a disbeliever.' And among them are those who reached the extreme of foolishness and shamelessness, claiming that the assertion of increase of attributes (ziyādat al-ṣifāt), and the permissibility of vision (ruʾya), and the emergence from the Fire, and that evils and ugly acts are by His creation, will, and intention, and the permissibility of manifesting a miracle upon the hand of a liar — all of it is disbelief.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage defines the issue of excommunication (takfīr) among Islamic sects. It first distinguishes between essential doctrines of Islam (ḍarūriyyāt al-islām) — where disagreement is unanimously considered disbelief — and secondary principles (uṣūl siwāhā) where the truth is one but disagreement may not be disbelief. It then presents the positions of major scholars: al-Ashʿarī and most Ashʿarīs hold the dissenter is not a disbeliever; al-Shāfiʿī's statement about not rejecting testimony of people of innovation (except the Khaṭṭābiyya) suggests leniency; Abū Ḥanīfa reportedly did not excommunicate anyone; some of their followers did excommunicate; the early Muʿtazila excommunicated those who affirm eternal attributes and the creation of actions, and considered the Jabriyya disbelievers, with al-Jubbāʾī taking an extreme position; and some extremists deemed many beliefs (increase of attributes, beatific vision, exit from hell, creation of evil, miracles on liars) as kufr.

Key Terms
ضروريات الإسلام
أصول سواها
تكفير المخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the central question being discussed?
A) Are the necessities of Islam known?
B) Does disagreement on secondary principles constitute disbelief?
C) Are the attributes of God eternal?
D) Is the world created?

Key Takeaway
The issue of excommunication hinges on a distinction between essential doctrines (ḍarūriyyāt) and disputed principles (uṣūl siwāhā), with only the former allowing unanimous takfīr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a theological point you hold strongly, such as the permissibility of seeing God in the afterlife. If a fellow Muslim disagreed with you, would you be tempted to question their faith? What does this lesson's distinction between essentials and disputed principles tell you about that impulse?
?
Have you ever judged someone's Islam based on a secondary issue that scholars themselves differed on? How would the scholars in this passage view your judgment?
?
If you truly believed that only the necessities of Islam are required for faith, how would that change the way you interact with Muslims who differ on matters like qadar or the attributes of Allah? Are you willing to accept that they may be wrong but still be your brothers and sisters in faith?

Scholarly Positions on Excommunication in Secondary Issues
Enumeration of scholarly positions on excommunication in secondary matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of scholarly opinions on excommunicating those who disagree on secondary theological principles, from Ashʿarī's leniency to Muʿtazilī and extremist strictness.

Core Idea
Scholars split sharply on takfīr; most Ashʿarīs and Ḥanafīs avoided it, while early Muʿtazila and some extremists deemed many beliefs kufr.

After framing the issue, the passage now presents the actual positions held by major scholars and schools. It moves from the more lenient stance of al-Ashʿarī and most of his followers, supported by indications from al-Shāfiʿī and Abū Ḥanīfa, to the harsh excommunication by the early Muʿtazila and even more extreme views. This lesson surveys that spectrum.

Guided Path
1
Read the positions of Ashʿarī, Shāfiʿī, and Abū Ḥanīfa
Notice that Ashʿarī and most Ashʿarīs said the dissenter is not a disbeliever. Al-Shāfiʿī's statement about not rejecting testimony (except Khaṭṭābiyya) and Abū Ḥanīfa's reported view align with leniency.
The phrase 'به يشعر' indicates implicit evidence.
2
Contrast with the Muʿtazilī position
The early Muʿtazila excommunicated opponents on attributes and actions, and al-Jubbāʾī's extreme chain of takfīr shows a very strict stance.
The Jabriyya are singled out for condemnation.
3
Examine the extremist list
Some went further, declaring many beliefs (increase of attributes, beatific vision, creation of evil) as kufr. This shows the range of rigor.
The author criticizes this as 'foolishness and shamelessness.'

In the chapter of disbelief (kufr) and faith (īmān): Its meaning is that those who agree upon what is among the necessities of Islam (ḍarūriyyāt al-islām) — such as the originatedness of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam), and the resurrection of bodies (ḥashr al-ajsād), and what resembles that — and they differ upon other principles (uṣūl siwāhā) such as the question of attributes, and the creation of actions, and the universality of will, and the pre-eternity of speech, and the permissibility of vision (of God), and the like of that, concerning which there is no dispute that the truth is one — does the one who opposes the truth by that belief and by speaking it become a disbeliever (kāfir) or not? Otherwise, there is no dispute about the disbelief of the people of the qibla who, while persisting throughout life in acts of obedience, come to believe in the pre-eternity of the world, and the denial of resurrection, and the denial of knowledge of particulars, and the like of that. And likewise regarding the occurrence of something from the causes of disbelief (mūjibāt al-kufr) from him. As for what we have mentioned, al-Shaykh al-Ashʿarī and most of the followers (al-aṣḥāb) went that he is not a disbeliever, and what al-Shāfiʿī (may God have mercy on him) said indicates it: 'I do not reject the testimony of the people of desires except the Khaṭṭābiyya, because they deem lying permissible.' And in al-Muntaqā from Abū Ḥanīfa (may God have mercy on him) that he did not excommunicate anyone from the people of the qibla, and upon that are most of the jurists. And among our companions are those who said the disbelief of the opponents. And the early Muʿtazila said the disbelief of those who affirm the eternal attributes (al-ṣifāt al-qadīma), and the creation of actions (khalq al-aʿmāl), and the disbelief of the Jabriyya (al-mujbira), so much so that it was narrated from al-Jubbāʾī that he said: 'The Jabari is a disbeliever. Commentary
This passage defines the issue of excommunication (takfīr) among Islamic sects. It first distinguishes between essential doctrines of Islam (ḍarūriyyāt al-islām) — where disagreement is unanimously considered disbelief — and secondary principles (uṣūl siwāhā) where the truth is one but disagreement may not be disbelief. Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is al-Ashʿarī's position on excommunicating one who disagrees on secondary principles?
A) He is a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
The majority of early Sunni scholars (Ashʿarī, Shāfiʿī, Abū Ḥanīfa) did not excommunicate those who erred on secondary theological principles, in contrast to Muʿtazilī and extremist positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been certain that someone else’s theological error takes them out of Islam? Reflect on Al-Jubbāʾī’s chain: if you doubt their disbelief, are you yourself a disbeliever? Does that possibility humble you or frighten you? Commit now to checking your own heart before judging another’s faith.
?
The extremists in the passage declared core Sunni beliefs like the vision of Allah and the increase of attributes as kufr. Is there any belief you hold that you consider so essential that denying it would make someone a disbeliever? How does the mainstream Sunni reluctance to excommunicate challenge your readiness to label others?
?
The early scholars (Abū Ḥanīfa, al-Shāfiʿī) were slow to excommunicate. Are you quicker to judge than they were? What concrete step can you take this week to give a fellow Muslim the benefit of the doubt in a matter of theological disagreement?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
8 min
Al-Isfara'ini and Al-Razi on Excommunication
Al-Isfara'ini's and al-Razi's views on excommunication with argument and distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of al-Isfara'ini and al-Razi on excommunication of Muslims who disagree on disputed theological principles, and al-Razi's main argument against excommunication.

Core Idea
Excommunication is not easy: al-Razi argues that disputed principles do not warrant takfir.

In this passage, the author presents two contrasting views on excommunication: al-Isfara'ini's conditional approach and al-Razi's more tolerant stance. Al-Razi's argument relies on the Prophet's practice and a distinction between clear and hidden principles. This lesson explores these views and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage moves from stating views to arguments to answers.
2
Identify al-Isfara'ini's and al-Razi's positions
Find the two different rulings: al-Isfara'ini says he excommunicates those who excommunicate him; al-Razi says no one from the people of the qibla is excommunicated.
Al-Razi's position is more inclusive.
3
Analyze al-Razi's argument and distinction
Al-Razi argues that if disputed principles were essential, the Prophet would have demanded explicit belief. Then he distinguishes between agreed-upon principles (like resurrection) which are clear, and disputed principles which are hidden.
This distinction is key to his reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الاستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرائني بكفر من يكفرنا، ومن لا فلا. واختيار الإمام الرازي أنه لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة. وتمسك بأنه لو توقف صحة الإسلام على اعتقاد الحق في تلك الأصول لكان النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) ومن بعده يطالبون بها من آمن، ويفتشون عن عقائدهم فيها . وينبهونهم على ما هو الحق منها. واللازم منتف قطعا ثم فرق بينها وبين ما هو من أصول الإسلام بالاتفاق بأن بعضها مما اشتهر كونه من الدين ، واشتمل عليه الكتاب بحيث لا يحتاج إلى البيان . كحشر الأجساد، وبعضها مما ظهرت أدلتها على ما يليق بأصحاب الجمل بحيث يتسارع إليها الإفهام، كحدوث العالم. وإنما طال الكلام فيها لإزالة شكوك الفقهاء المبطلون، بخلاف الأصول الخلافية، فإن الحق فيها خفي يفتقر إلى زيادة نظر وتأمل، والكتاب والسنة قد يشتملان على ما يتخيل معارضاً لحجة أهل الحق . فلو كانت مخالفة الحق فيها كفراً ، لا حتيج الى البيان البتة . ثم أجاب عن أدلة تكفير الفرق بعضهم بعضاً بأجوبة مبني بعضها على أن خرق الإجماع ليس بكفر، وأن الإجماع لا ينعقد بدون اتفاق المشبهة والمجسمة والروافض وأمثالهم . وبعضها على أن من لزمه الكفر، ولم يقل به فليس بكافر. وبعضها على أن صاحب التأويل - وإن كان ظاهر البطلان - ليس بكافر. ووافقه بعض المتأخرين من المعتزلة حذراً عن شفاعة تكفير من تكاد تشهد الأرض والسماء بإسلامهم، وعن لزوم تكفير كثير من كبارهم ، لكن كلامهم يموج بتكفير عظماء أهل الإسلام ، والله عزيز ذو انتقام .ولقائل أن يجيب تمسك الإمام بمنع الملازمة بأن التصديق بجميع ما جاء به النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) إجمالاً كاف في صحة الإيمان، وإنما يحتاج الى بيان الحق في التفاصيل عند ملاحظتها، وإن كانت مما لا خلاف في تكفير المخالف فيها، كحدوث العالم، فكم من مؤمن لم يعرف معنى الحادث والقديم (۲) (۳) أصلا، ولم يخطر بباله حديث حشر الأجساد قطعاً. لكن إذا لاحظ ذلك، فلو لم يصدق كان كافراً.
And al-Ustādh Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāʾinī said: The disbelief of the one who declares us disbelievers, and whoever does not, then not. And the choice of Imām al-Rāzī is that he does not excommunicate anyone from the people of the qibla. He argued that if the correctness of Islam depended on believing the truth in those principles (uṣūl), then the Prophet (peace be upon him) and those after him would have demanded them from those who believed, and would have inquired about their creeds concerning them, and would have alerted them to what is the truth among them. But the consequence is negated definitively. Then he distinguished between them and what is among the principles of Islam by agreement: that some of them are such that it is well-known that they are from the religion, and the Book includes them such that they do not need explanation — like the resurrection of bodies; and some of them are such that their proofs appear in a manner befitting the masses so that the understanding hastens to them — like the originatedness of the world. But the speech is prolonged concerning them only to remove the doubts of the corrupting jurists, unlike the disputed principles (al-uṣūl al-khilāfiyya), for the truth concerning them is hidden, requiring additional reflection and contemplation, and the Book and the Sunnah may include what is imagined to be contradictory to the proof of the people of truth. So if opposing the truth in them were disbelief, there would be no need for explanation at all. Then he answered the proofs of the excommunication of sects by one another with answers, some of them based on the fact that breaking consensus (kharq al-ijmāʿ) is not disbelief, and that consensus is not formed without the agreement of the Mushabbiha, Mujassima, Rawāfiḍ, and their likes. And some of them based on the fact that the one upon whom disbelief is incumbent but who does not say it is not a disbeliever. And some of them based on the fact that the one who makes an interpretation (taʾwīl) — even if it is apparently false — is not a disbeliever. And some later Muʿtazilites agreed with him, cautious about the intercession of excommunication against those whom the earth and sky almost witness to their Islam, and about the necessity of excommunicating many of their great figures. But their speech surges with excommunication of the great ones of the people of Islam — and God is Almighty, Avenger. And it is possible for one to answer, by way of objecting to the Imām's argument by denying the implication (manʿ al-mulāzama), that the affirmation (taṣdīq) of everything that the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought, in summary (ijmālan), is sufficient for the correctness of faith (īmān). Detailed explanation (bayān al-ḥaqq fī al-tafāṣīl) is only needed when considering the details, even if they are among those where there is no dispute about excommunicating the opponent — such as the originatedness of the world. For how many a believer does not know the meaning of the originated (al-ḥādith) and the eternal (al-qadīm) at all, and has never come to mind the matter of the resurrection of bodies at all. But if he considers that, then if he did not believe, he would be a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents the views of al-Isfarāʾinī and al-Rāzī on excommunication, followed by a counter-argument. Al-Isfarāʾinī says he excommunicates those who excommunicate him, but not otherwise. Al-Rāzī holds that no one from the people of the qibla is a disbeliever. Al-Rāzī's argument is that if the validity of Islam depended on correct belief in those disputed principles, the Prophet would have demanded such belief explicitly — yet he did not. He then distinguishes between principles that are unanimously agreed upon (like resurrection) whose proofs are clear, and disputed principles where the truth is hidden. He also answers proofs of excommunication by noting that breaking consensus is not kufr, consensus requires including deviant groups, someone forced into kufr but not saying it is not a disbeliever, and false interpretation does not necessarily entail disbelief. Some later Muʿtazilites agreed with him, but their language still excommunicates great Muslims. The counter-argument objects that implicit affirmation of all that the Prophet brought is sufficient for faith; detailed belief is only required when the details are considered. Many believers have no concept of 'originated' or 'eternal' or resurrection, but if they were to consider and disbelieve, they would be disbelievers.

Key Terms
أبو إسحاق الإسفرائني
الإمام الرازي
أصول خلافية
خرق الإجماع
صاحب التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is al-Rāzī's main argument for not excommunicating those who disagree on disputed principles?
A) The Prophet didn't demand explicit belief in them.
B) Consensus forbids excommunication.
C) They are not Muslims.
D) The Qur'an is unclear.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's position is that no Muslim should be excommunicated solely for differing on disputed theological principles, based on the Prophet's practice and the hidden nature of such principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even silently in your heart, considered someone's faith invalid because they differed from you on a theological issue? How does al-Razi's argument—that the Prophet himself did not demand explicit belief in such matters—expose the arrogance of your judgment? What will you do today to uproot that habit?
?
The passage says some later Mu'tazilites outwardly agreed with al-Razi but their speech still excommunicated great figures. Where in your own words or thoughts do you find yourself doing the same—cautious in principle but quick to condemn in practice? Confess that hypocrisy and commit to changing your inner disposition.
?
Al-Razi distinguishes between principles whose truth is clear and those that are hidden, requiring deep reflection. Are you honestly willing to admit that some of your own strongly held beliefs might fall into the latter category? How can you actively seek to understand the reasons of those you disagree with, rather than dismissing or condemning them?
